| Books on OS implementation by non-kernel-developers | Shallow despite technical competence | Skip with caution |

### A note on contemporary relevance

Operating systems as a research field is not dead, but its center of gravity has shifted. The frontier work is at the edges: in formal verification (seL4 and successors, with related projects exploring formally verified hypervisors and trusted execution environments), in unikernels and library operating systems (which question whether the conventional kernel-application boundary is the right one for cloud-era workloads), in operating-systems support for serverless and confidential computing, in the operating-systems aspects of accelerator integration (GPUs, TPUs, and the broader ecosystem of specialized processors), and in operating-systems support for AI workloads.

The post-cloud-era reshaping of operating systems is the most consequential contemporary development. Cloud computing has not eliminated the need for operating-systems competence; it has redistributed it. Operating-systems concerns now appear at multiple layers: the host operating system running on the underlying hardware, the guest operating systems running in virtual machines, the container-isolation mechanisms running between processes, the language-runtime mechanisms that approximate operating-systems functions for managed-language applications. Each of these layers requires the worldview that operating systems develops, and engineers who lack that worldview struggle at all of them.

The security crisis in operating systems is ongoing and deepening. Memory-safety vulnerabilities account for the majority of serious security flaws in operating systems written in C, and the response — gradual migration to memory-safe languages (Rust, primarily) for new operating-systems code, hardware-supported security extensions, formal verification of critical components — is reshaping how operating-systems work is done. Microsoft's commitment to Rust for new Windows kernel components, the Rust-for-Linux project, and the various academic operating systems written in Rust represent a substantial bet that the next generation of operating-systems work will be done with stronger safety guarantees than the current generation provides. Whether this bet pays off is one of the most consequential open questions about the field's near future.

The relationship between operating systems and machine learning is at an early and uncertain stage. Operating systems are increasingly running machine-learning workloads, and the resource-management mechanisms designed for general-purpose workloads are not always well-suited to the patterns of machine-learning training and inference. There is also early work on using machine learning *within* operating systems — for scheduling decisions, cache replacement, prefetching — though the practical results so far are limited. Whether machine learning becomes a serious component of operating-systems design is open.

Despite these contemporary developments, the foundational character of operating systems is robust. The four commitments described above — to the reality of resources, to the inevitability of failure, to the primacy of contention, to leak-aware abstraction — apply as much to cloud computing as to traditional operating systems, as much to confidential computing as to conventional computing, as much to operating systems for accelerators as to operating systems for CPUs. A learner who masters the foundational material is equipped to engage with whatever specific developments emerge, and the foundational material is what the canonical texts cover. The contemporary developments add to the foundational picture but do not replace it.

## 2.6 — Computer Networks

### What it is, properly

The conventional account of computer networks treats the subject as the study of how computers communicate with one another — a study organized around protocol stacks, layered architectures, and specific technologies (Ethernet, IP, TCP, HTTP). This account is not wrong, but it captures the surface of the subject without conveying what the subject is actually about. A student who completes a course taught at this level acquires familiarity with protocol vocabulary and layer diagrams without grasping the deeper intellectual content, and that intellectual content is what justifies the subject's place in the foundational trunk.

A more honest account begins by recognizing that computer networks is the subject in which the assumptions implicit in single-machine operating systems are tested against the realities of distributed communication, and in which the conceptual framework for thinking about systems composed of independent communicating machines is established. Single-machine operating systems assume reliable shared memory, synchronous clock semantics, predictable failure modes, and trusted internal communication. Networks remove every one of these assumptions: communication between machines is unreliable, asynchronous, partially ordered, susceptible to eavesdropping and tampering, and bounded by physical constants that do not constrain communication within a single machine. The intellectual content of networks is the body of techniques for building reliable communication on top of an unreliable substrate, for coordinating distributed actions in the absence of shared memory, and for designing protocols that scale to billions of participants and remain functional across decades of evolution.

The subject is, viewed this way, fundamentally about the design of distributed systems — and indeed networks is the foundation on which distributed systems (Section 2.9) builds. The distinction between the two subjects is partially historical and partially methodological: networks focuses on the communication substrate and the protocols that run directly on it, while distributed systems focuses on the higher-level coordination problems that arise once communication is given. The boundary between the subjects is not sharp, and serious treatments of either substantially engage with the other. The placement in the foundational trunk reflects the fact that some understanding of networks is required before distributed systems becomes approachable, not that networks is a more elementary subject.

The content of networks, examined seriously, is structured around three layers of conceptual difficulty. The first layer is the *physical and link layer*: the mechanisms by which signals are transmitted between adjacent devices and by which devices on a shared medium coordinate their access to that medium. This layer is partly electrical engineering and partly computer science, and the computer-science content is largely about the algorithms that govern shared access (Ethernet's CSMA/CD, the various wireless coordination schemes, the link-layer error-detection codes). The second layer is the *internetwork layer*: the design of protocols that route packets across networks of networks, with the Internet Protocol as the central example. This layer is where the conceptual achievement of "the network of networks" is realized — the recognition that diverse underlying networks can be united by a common addressing scheme and routing protocol, and that this unification produces a global communication infrastructure that none of its constituent networks could provide alone. The third layer is the *transport and application layer*: the design of protocols that provide reliable, secure, and useful communication services on top of the unreliable internetwork layer. This is where TCP, TLS, HTTP, DNS, and the rest of the Internet's protocol suite live, and it is where the design choices most visible to working engineers are made.

### The deep problems the field tries to answer

Three problems organize computer networks as a discipline.

**The problem of communication over an unreliable substrate.** The physical world does not provide reliable communication. Wires fail, wireless signals fade, packets are dropped, bits are corrupted, machines fail to acknowledge what they have received, and entire communication paths become unavailable. The naive response — building reliable physical communication — has been pursued and has limits: physical reliability is expensive to engineer and never absolute. The actual response of the field has been to accept unreliable physical communication as given and to construct reliability at higher layers through retransmission, acknowledgment, error detection, and timeout-based recovery. The intellectual achievement is the recognition that reliability is not a property of the underlying medium but a service constructed on top of it, and that this construction can be done with predictable cost and bounded complexity. The mechanisms — sliding-window protocols, the various forms of automatic repeat request, congestion control algorithms — are the technical content of this recognition.

**The problem of scale.** The Internet now connects billions of devices and trillions of communications per day. A network protocol that worked for a hundred machines might fail for a million; one that worked for a million might fail for a billion. The design of protocols that scale gracefully to extreme sizes is genuinely difficult, and many of the most consequential decisions in the Internet's history have been driven by scaling considerations. The hierarchical address structure of IP, the distributed routing protocols that maintain routing tables without central coordination, the caching infrastructure of DNS, the content delivery networks that moved popular content closer to its consumers — all of these are responses to the scale problem. Working at scale requires giving up properties that small-scale systems can take for granted (consistency, complete knowledge, reliable delivery) and replacing them with weaker but more achievable properties (eventual consistency, partial knowledge, best-effort delivery). The intellectual content of the scale problem is the body of techniques for making this trade-off well, and it is what allows the Internet to function despite being too large for any of its participants to fully understand.

**The problem of evolution.** The Internet has been operating continuously since the early 1980s, with major aspects of its design unchanged across that period. The original Internet Protocol specification (IPv4) is still the dominant protocol for most Internet traffic, though it has been augmented and extended. The original Domain Name System design from 1983 is essentially unchanged in its core mechanisms. TCP has been refined extensively but its fundamental design is from the 1970s. This longevity is unprecedented in computing, and it is not accidental: the protocols were designed with explicit attention to allowing future evolution without requiring synchronized changes across the entire network. The mechanisms — version fields, optional parameters, layered architecture, the end-to-end principle — are all responses to the evolution problem. The intellectual content is the recognition that protocols deployed at scale cannot be redesigned from scratch, and must instead be designed for graceful evolution from their initial deployment forward. This recognition shapes how serious protocol design is conducted, and it explains why the protocols that have worked best are often not the cleanest from a pure-design perspective.

### Where it sits in the map

Computer networks is the third subject of the systems triad. It presupposes operating systems (Section 2.5), because most of what networks does happens within operating-system kernels and is exposed to applications through operating-system interfaces. It is presupposed by distributed systems (Section 2.9), which builds the higher-level coordination infrastructure on top of network communication. The triad of architecture, operating systems, and networks is the engineering foundation of computer science, and competent work in any one of them depends on at least working knowledge of the other two.

The relationship to distributed systems deserves explicit treatment. Networks and distributed systems are sometimes presented as a single combined subject, and there are intellectual arguments for this presentation. The decision in this document to treat them separately reflects a pedagogical judgment: networks is approached productively at the level of specific protocols and layered architectures, while distributed systems is approached productively at the level of consensus algorithms, replication protocols, and consistency models. The two levels are connected, but engaging with both simultaneously without first having mastered either tends to produce confusion. The recommendation is to study networks first, gaining fluency with the substrate on which distributed systems builds, before approaching distributed systems.

The relationship to operating systems is direct. Network protocols are implemented in operating-system kernels, and the performance and correctness of networking is bound up with operating-system concerns: scheduling, memory management, interrupt handling, system-call overhead. Many of the most consequential developments in modern networking — kernel bypass, zero-copy networking, programmable network interfaces, eBPF-based packet processing — are simultaneously networking developments and operating-systems developments, and the boundary between the two subjects is increasingly contested.

The relationship to programming is more variable than for other systems subjects. Network programming at the application level is a routine activity for most software engineers, and most engineers have substantial network-programming experience even if they have not formally studied networks. But this practical experience tends to be shallow: engineers learn to make HTTP requests, to write socket-based servers, to use library-provided abstractions, without engaging with the deeper layers of protocol design and implementation. The trunk-level study of networks is what converts shallow practical familiarity into deep understanding, and the conversion is what justifies the time invested.

The position within the four axes of Chapter 1 is primarily on the system axis, with substantial engagement on the data axis (because networks is fundamentally about moving data between systems) and indirect engagement on the computation axis (through the algorithmic content of routing, congestion control, and protocol design). The intelligence axis is barely present, except in recent and somewhat speculative work on machine-learning-based traffic classification, congestion control, and routing.

### What you become after studying it

A reader who has internalized computer networks acquires several capacities that distinguish them from engineers who treat the network as a black box.

The first is *the ability to reason about distributed communication*. The single-machine assumptions that most programmers rely on — shared memory, synchronous calls, reliable communication — do not hold across networks, and reasoning about programs that communicate across networks requires a different conceptual framework. The networks-trained engineer thinks naturally about partial failure (a remote call may not return because the request was lost, the response was lost, or the remote machine crashed mid-operation, and these cases are not distinguishable from the caller's perspective), about timing (calls take variable amounts of time and may exceed timeouts even when they will eventually succeed), and about ordering (events on different machines have no inherent ordering, and any ordering must be constructed by the protocol designer). This conceptual framework is what makes distributed-systems work approachable, and it is acquired through extended engagement with networking material.

The second capacity is *fluency with the layered abstraction*. The networks layered model (the OSI seven-layer model, or the more practical TCP/IP four-layer model) is initially encountered as a set of vocabulary to be memorized, and many engineers retain this surface-level understanding throughout their careers. The mature understanding is different: the layers are a working tool for thinking about protocols, allowing the engineer to reason about each layer's concerns in isolation while remembering how the layers compose. When debugging a connectivity problem, the layered framework provides the systematic decomposition: is this a physical connectivity issue, a link-layer issue, a routing issue, a transport issue, or an application issue? Each layer has characteristic failure modes and characteristic diagnostic techniques, and the layered framework structures the diagnostic process. Engineers without this framework debug network problems by trying things until something works; engineers with it debug systematically.

The third capacity is *the discipline of protocol design*. Most engineers will not design protocols professionally, but the discipline of protocol design teaches habits of thought that transfer to many other design problems. A protocol designer must specify behavior precisely enough that independent implementations interoperate, must anticipate failure modes that the protocol's participants may face, must consider how the protocol will evolve over time, and must balance competing concerns (performance, complexity, security, robustness). These habits of thought — precise specification, failure-mode analysis, evolutionary design, balanced tradeoffs — are valuable far beyond protocol design itself, and they are most clearly taught through networks material.

The fourth capacity is *security awareness at the network level*. Networks is one of the primary loci of computer security, and the security history of the Internet is largely the history of attacks on network protocols and the corresponding defenses. Understanding TLS, the various authentication mechanisms, the security implications of network protocols (DNS, BGP, the email protocols), the categories of network-level attacks (denial of service, man-in-the-middle, traffic analysis), and the trade-offs in defending against them is essential for any engineer working on systems that communicate over networks — which is to say, almost any engineer working on contemporary software. The networks-trained engineer can read security advisories and reason about whether their systems are affected; the untrained engineer must rely on others to make this judgment.

The fifth capacity is *the ability to reason about scale*. Working with networks at the level the trunk subject treats means engaging with mechanisms that scale to billions of devices, and the scaling considerations are visible in the design of every major protocol. Engineers who have studied this material develop instincts for scale that engineers without it lack: which design choices admit growth gracefully, which create bottlenecks that emerge only at scale, which require changes that are easy at small scale but impossible at large scale. These instincts shape how the trained engineer designs new systems even when scale is not yet a concern, because the design choices made early often determine whether scale is achievable later.

### How the field sees the world

Computer networks embeds a worldview that distinguishes it from adjacent systems disciplines, and that has remained stable enough to survive the technology generations the field has experienced.

The first commitment is *to the impossibility of reliable communication*. The networks worldview takes for granted that communication between distinct machines is fundamentally unreliable, that this unreliability cannot be eliminated by engineering effort but can only be managed through protocols that operate on top of unreliable substrates, and that any system that assumes reliable communication will fail in practice. This commitment runs deep: it shapes how networks-trained engineers think about retries (which are necessary), about timeouts (which are unavoidable), about idempotency (which is essential when retries are present), and about partial failure (which is the dominant failure mode of distributed systems). Engineers without this commitment treat communication failures as exceptional cases to be handled defensively; engineers with it treat communication success as the local result of protocols designed to produce success on top of failure.

The second commitment is *to the end-to-end principle*. This principle, articulated in the 1981 paper by Saltzer, Reed, and Clark that has become foundational to network design, holds that functions can be implemented correctly only at the endpoints of a communication, and that intermediate nodes should provide only the minimum services required to allow endpoint communication. The principle is partly empirical (functions implemented at intermediate nodes typically must be re-implemented at endpoints anyway, making the intermediate implementation redundant) and partly architectural (a network whose intermediate nodes are simple is easier to evolve, more reliable, and more general than a network whose intermediate nodes are complex). The end-to-end principle has been contested and qualified over the decades, but it remains one of the central commitments of network design, and its influence is visible in the success of the Internet over the more elaborate alternatives that competed with it.

The third commitment is *to the dumb-network/smart-endpoint architecture*. This is a corollary of the end-to-end principle, but it deserves separate articulation because of its consequences. The Internet is an unusually dumb network: its core function is to forward packets, and its core intelligence resides at the endpoints (in TCP, in TLS, in HTTP). This dumb-core/smart-edge architecture is what allows the Internet to support arbitrary applications without requiring the network to be modified, which is why new applications (the Web, video streaming, peer-to-peer file sharing, real-time communication) can be deployed without coordinated changes to the underlying infrastructure. The architecture has costs — a smarter network could provide better service for some applications — but the benefits in evolvability and generality have been overwhelming, and the architecture has shaped how serious network engineers think about design choices throughout the stack.

The fourth commitment is *to standards as primary artifacts*. Networks is unusual among CS subjects in that its primary artifacts are not implementations but standards: the RFC documents that specify Internet protocols, the various standards published by the IEEE for link-layer protocols, the W3C standards for Web protocols. These documents are the actual specifications of the protocols, and any implementation that deviates from them is non-compliant. The networks-trained engineer treats these documents seriously, knows how to read them, and consults them when implementations disagree about expected behavior. Engineers without this orientation treat protocols as defined by their popular implementations, which works most of the time but fails when implementations disagree or when novel situations arise. The discipline of treating standards as primary is one of the distinctive contributions of networks education.

These four commitments — to the unreliability of communication, to the end-to-end principle, to dumb-network/smart-endpoint architecture, and to standards as primary artifacts — distinguish the networks worldview from adjacent worldviews. A practitioner who has internalized them sees networks as a coherent design philosophy rather than as a collection of protocols, and the coherence is what allows them to extrapolate from existing protocols to new situations.

### Common pitfalls in learning it

The pitfalls of learning computer networks are characteristic and well-documented, and the most damaging are particular to the subject's combination of theoretical content and practical immediacy.

The most common pitfall is *layer-diagram fixation*. Networks is taught using layered models (most commonly the OSI seven-layer model or the TCP/IP four-layer model), and these models are useful for organizing the subject. They become obstacles when learners treat them as the subject itself, memorizing the layers and the protocols that occupy each without engaging with what the layers are actually doing. A learner who can recite the seven OSI layers but cannot explain what makes the transport layer's concerns different from the network layer's concerns has memorized vocabulary without understanding. The countermeasure is to focus, during study, on the conceptual content of each layer — what problem the layer addresses, what techniques it uses, what failures it handles — rather than on the names and the diagrams.

A second pitfall is *protocol-detail overload*. Networks involves many specific protocols, each with substantial detail (header formats, state machines, timer values, error codes). Learners often try to memorize this detail and find themselves overwhelmed. The detail matters in professional practice — debugging a TCP connectivity problem requires knowing the TCP state machine — but it matters at the level of being able to look it up and apply it, not at the level of pure memorization. The countermeasure is to focus during initial learning on the conceptual structure of the major protocols (what TCP is for, what it does, what its main mechanisms are) and to treat the specific details as reference material to be consulted when needed.

A third pitfall is *the practical-immediacy trap*. Networks is one of the few CS subjects where students arrive with substantial practical experience: they have used the Internet daily for years, configured home networks, and made HTTP requests in their programs. This experience can produce the illusion of understanding the underlying material, leading learners to skim the conceptual content because they "already know" how networks work. The skimming produces shallow learning that does not survive contact with the kinds of problems serious network work involves. The countermeasure is to recognize that practical familiarity with using the Internet is not understanding of how it works, and to engage with the conceptual content as if encountering it for the first time.

A fourth pitfall is *neglecting the security dimension*. Networks security is genuinely difficult and is treated, in most introductory texts, as a separate chapter near the end of the book. Learners often skim or skip this material under time pressure, with the result that their networks understanding is incomplete in a way that matters for almost all professional work. The countermeasure is to treat networks security as integral to networks rather than as an optional extension, and to extend study through serious engagement with the security literature on networking specifically.

A fifth pitfall is *the academic-versus-engineering gap*. Networks textbooks often present protocols as they are formally specified, while production systems often deviate from the formal specifications in subtle ways. The differences include implementation bugs that have become de facto standards (because too much code depends on them to fix), optimizations that work in practice but are not in the standard, and extensions that have not been formally specified. Engineers who learn only the textbook version are surprised when they encounter production systems and find them not behaving as expected. The countermeasure is to supplement textbook study with engagement with how protocols are actually implemented in major systems — the Linux kernel networking stack, the standard implementations of HTTP servers, the major DNS resolvers — and with the operational lore that documents the differences between standards and reality.

A sixth pitfall is *the cloud-abstraction trap*. Many contemporary engineers encounter networks primarily through cloud abstractions (load balancers, managed databases, content delivery networks, service meshes) and may conclude that the underlying network concepts are no longer relevant. This conclusion is wrong. The cloud abstractions are themselves built on network primitives, the failure modes of cloud systems are typically network failure modes one or two layers removed, and serious cloud engineering requires the same network competence as on-premises engineering. The countermeasure is to recognize that cloud computing is networks extended rather than networks replaced, and to study the underlying material accordingly.

A seventh pitfall is *the IPv4-only mindset*. The Internet is in the middle of a long transition from IPv4 to IPv6, and the transition is now decades old and still incomplete. Learners who study only IPv4 acquire a picture of the Internet that was accurate twenty years ago and is increasingly inaccurate today. The countermeasure is to study both protocols, to understand why the transition has been so prolonged, and to develop mental models that accommodate the dual-protocol reality that contemporary networks operate in.

### The literature of the subject

Computer networks has a stable but not entirely settled literature. The major textbooks have been competing for primacy for decades, with no single text occupying the dominant position that OSTEP holds in operating systems or that Sipser holds in the theory of computation. The selections below cover the main options and the appropriate sequence for serious learning.

#### Canonical entry points

The most widely used contemporary entry to computer networks is Kurose and Ross's *Computer Networking: A Top-Down Approach*. The book's distinctive feature is its top-down organization: it begins with the application layer (with which students have direct experience) and works downward to the physical layer, rather than building up from physical signals to application protocols. This pedagogical choice has substantial benefits for self-learners — concepts are introduced in a context that motivates them, and the abstraction layer being studied at any given moment is connected to recognizable applications. The writing is unusually clear for systems literature, the worked examples are pedagogically useful, and the associated programming assignments (particularly the well-known socket programming labs) are effective. For self-study, Kurose and Ross is the standard contemporary choice.

The classical alternative, with substantially different emphasis, is Tanenbaum and Wetherall's *Computer Networks*. This book is bottom-up rather than top-down, beginning with the physical and link layers and building up to applications. The bottom-up organization is the traditional approach and is preferred by some learners and instructors, particularly those with more electrical-engineering background or those who want a more thorough treatment of the lower layers. Tanenbaum's book is also more comprehensive than Kurose and Ross's, covering some topics (wireless networks, security, multimedia) more deeply. For learners who find the top-down approach uncongenial, Tanenbaum is the right alternative.

The deeper-than-introductory standard reference is *Computer Networks: A Systems Approach* by Peterson and Davie. The book is more focused on the systems-engineering aspects of networking — how protocols are actually implemented, how networks are actually operated — than the introductory texts are, and it serves as the bridge from textbook understanding to professional competence. Peterson and Davie is appropriate as a second text for serious learners, after one of the introductory texts has been completed.

The reading order recommendation: Kurose and Ross as the primary text for most learners, with Tanenbaum consulted for the chapters where its depth or different emphasis is valuable. Peterson and Davie after the introductory text, as the bridge to professional engagement with the subject.

The associated course materials are valuable. Stanford's CS 144 (Introduction to Computer Networking) is freely available and includes a substantial project — implementing a TCP/IP stack from scratch — that is among the most educational network programming exercises in any university curriculum. The CS 144 materials are an appropriate companion to Kurose and Ross and substantially extend what reading alone provides.

#### The progression

After the canonical introduction, the progression in networks branches according to specialization.

*TCP/IP at depth.* The standard reference for deep engagement with the TCP/IP protocol family is Stevens's *TCP/IP Illustrated, Volume 1*. Stevens's book treats the protocols at the level of detail required for serious implementation work, with extensive discussion of how the protocols actually behave under various conditions (rather than just how they are formally specified). The book's first edition, from 1994, was definitive for a generation of network engineers; the second edition, updated in 2011 by Fall and Stevens posthumously, brings the material current with developments through the 2000s. The book's depth makes it appropriate after the introductory texts, when the reader is prepared to engage with TCP/IP as a working engineer rather than as a student.

*Network programming at depth.* Stevens's other major work, *UNIX Network Programming, Volume 1: The Sockets Networking API* (third edition, 2003, with W. Richard Stevens, Bill Fenner, and Andrew M. Rudoff), is the standard reference for socket-based network programming. The book covers the Berkeley sockets API exhaustively, with attention to the details that production network programming requires. It is a reference rather than a textbook, but it is the right reference for any serious network-programming work in C.

*Modern protocols and their evolution.* The textbooks tend to lag behind the contemporary protocol landscape, particularly in the application layer. Specific protocols and protocol families that are important in contemporary practice but treated insufficiently in introductory texts include: HTTP/2 and HTTP/3 (which have largely replaced HTTP/1.1 in production); QUIC (which underlies HTTP/3 and represents a substantial departure from the TCP-based architecture that dominated for decades); modern TLS (TLS 1.3, which has substantially different design from earlier TLS versions); and the various protocols for real-time communication (WebRTC, the modern signaling protocols, the various media-transport protocols). The standard way to learn these protocols is to read the relevant RFCs and IETF drafts directly, supplemented by the technical writing of practitioners involved in their design. This is harder than reading textbooks but is the appropriate path for engineers who need contemporary competence.

*Routing at depth.* The treatment of routing in introductory texts is necessarily limited. For learners interested in network operations, Internet architecture, or the operational aspects of running large networks, deeper engagement with routing protocols (BGP in particular) is appropriate. The standard text is *Routing TCP/IP* by Doyle and Carroll (two volumes), which covers Internet routing protocols at the level of detail required for professional network engineering. This is operations-oriented material rather than computer-science-research material, and it is appropriate primarily for engineers planning to work in network operations or in companies that operate large networks.

*Wireless networks.* The treatment of wireless networking in general-purpose texts is variable. For learners specifically interested in wireless, the standard reference is Goldsmith's *Wireless Communications*, which is more electrical-engineering than computer-science but is the right entry to the wireless side of the field. Tanenbaum's general text covers wireless more deeply than Kurose and Ross, and is the right primary text for learners who want to integrate wireless concerns into general networks study without going into electrical-engineering depth.

#### Reference works

*TCP/IP Illustrated, Volume 1* (Stevens) and *UNIX Network Programming* (Stevens et al.), mentioned above as progression texts, are also primary references throughout one's career. Working network engineers consult these books regularly, and their position as standard references is unlikely to be displaced soon.

The RFCs themselves are the authoritative references for Internet protocols. Most working network engineers consult RFCs directly when implementations disagree or when behavior is not as expected, and the discipline of reading RFCs is one of the distinctive habits of network practice. The RFCs are freely available from the IETF, and they are searchable by the official RFC number or by the protocol they specify. The most important RFCs (RFC 791 on IP, RFC 793 on TCP, RFC 1034/1035 on DNS, RFC 2616 on HTTP/1.1, RFC 7540 on HTTP/2, RFC 9000 on QUIC, RFC 8446 on TLS 1.3) are appropriate reading for any serious network engineer.

The Linux kernel networking documentation and source code are the primary reference for how Internet protocols are actually implemented in the most widely-deployed networking stack. The documentation is uneven in quality, but it is authoritative, and reading the actual source code of the Linux network stack is one of the high-leverage activities for engineers seeking deep understanding of how networks actually work.

#### Heterodox correctives

The end-to-end principle paper (Saltzer, Reed, and Clark, "End-to-End Arguments in System Design," 1981) is foundational reading that should be encountered after introductory study. The paper articulates the architectural philosophy that has guided Internet design for decades, and reading it directly — rather than receiving its conclusions through textbook summaries — is illuminating. The paper is short and accessible, and it reveals the intellectual reasoning behind design choices that are usually presented as facts.

The various papers and books on the philosophy of network design are heterodox in the sense that they question the orthodoxies of contemporary networking. *Patterns in Network Architecture* by John Day argues that the layered model of networking has been misapplied and that a different architectural framework would be more coherent; the book is contrarian and not widely accepted, but engaging with it is illuminating for understanding what alternatives to the current architecture might look like. *RINA: Recursive InterNetwork Architecture*, also from Day, develops the alternative architecture in detail; it is a research vision rather than a deployed system, but it represents the most thorough alternative to the current Internet architecture that has been articulated.

The clean-slate Internet research (the GENI program, the various clean-slate architecture proposals from the 2000s and 2010s) is heterodox in a different sense: it accepts that the current Internet architecture has limitations but argues that incremental evolution cannot address them, and proposes redesigning from scratch. The proposals have not been adopted, but engaging with them is valuable for understanding what the limitations of the current Internet are and why they have been so resistant to change.

For the security dimension, the various papers and analyses of the major Internet vulnerabilities — the Morris worm of 1988, the BGP route hijacking incidents, the DNS cache poisoning attacks, the various TLS vulnerabilities — are essential reading. These document, often in detail, the security failures that have shaped how the Internet's security has evolved. The Mitnick-Shimomura saga (well-documented in *Takedown* and other books) provides the dramatic version of this history, and is engaging reading for learners who want narrative context for the otherwise dry security material.

#### What to skip and why

Most introductory networking books not mentioned above can be skipped if the canonical texts are available. The genre has converged on a few standard treatments, and minor variants offer little that is not better presented in Kurose and Ross or Tanenbaum.

Books that focus exclusively on particular vendor technologies (Cisco-certification books, Juniper-certification books, the various vendor-specific networking materials) should be skipped except by readers specifically pursuing those certifications. The vendor-specific material is professionally valuable in particular contexts but is not the right material for foundational learning, and engineers who learn networking through vendor materials acquire an incomplete picture of the field that is biased toward the vendor's specific design choices.

Books that promise to teach networking without engaging with the protocols (high-level conceptual introductions, "networking for managers" books) should be skipped by anyone serious about the subject. The protocols are the substance of the field, and treatments that omit them produce only surface familiarity.

Most online tutorials on networking topics are uneven in quality. The exceptions — Cloudflare's blog posts on HTTP and TLS, the various deep technical posts from the major CDN companies, Julia Evans's networking-related zines — are valuable, but the typical tutorial is too shallow to be worth the time investment compared to canonical texts.

The Cisco Networking Academy materials, while widely used, are oriented toward vendor certification rather than toward deep understanding. They are appropriate for engineers seeking Cisco certifications and inappropriate as primary learning material for anyone with broader goals.

#### Contemporary sources

Computer networks is an active field, and the contemporary sources are well-developed.

The IETF (Internet Engineering Task Force) is the primary body that develops Internet protocols, and engagement with the IETF process is one of the standard ways for working network engineers to stay current. The IETF publishes RFCs, hosts working groups for specific protocols, and runs three meetings per year (which are open to the public). Following the major working groups — the HTTP working group, the QUIC working group, the TLS working group — gives a sense of how protocol design is actually done.

The major networking conferences are SIGCOMM, NSDI, IMC, and CONEXT. SIGCOMM is the flagship conference and publishes the most influential research; NSDI is more systems-oriented; IMC focuses on Internet measurement; CONEXT is the European counterpart. Their proceedings are freely available and represent the research frontier of the field.

Several technical blogs are valuable for contemporary engagement. Cloudflare's blog covers contemporary networking developments at substantial technical depth. APNIC's blog covers Internet operations and policy. The various ISP and CDN engineering blogs (Fastly, Akamai, the various major operators) provide insight into how the Internet is actually run. Following these gives a sense of contemporary operational concerns that academic literature does not capture.

Wireshark is the standard tool for network packet analysis, and learning to use it is essentially mandatory for any serious networking work. The Wireshark documentation and the various tutorials on its use are valuable resources.

For contemporary academic perspective, the various survey papers in *ACM Computing Surveys* on networking topics are useful summaries of specific subfields. These tend to be written for researchers entering the area and provide more accessible entry to specific topics than reading individual papers.
