| SIGMOD, VLDB, ICDE, CIDR proceedings | Research frontier | Current, sampling |
| Andy Pavlo lectures and blog | Accessible contemporary database writing | Current, ongoing |
| Use The Index, Luke (Markus Winand) | Practical indexing reference | Current, reference |
| CMU and MIT database group blogs | Academic-research perspective | Current, ongoing |
| Cloud-provider database blogs (Snowflake, etc.) | Industry-engineering perspective | Current, ongoing |
| Product-specific certification books (Oracle, SQL Server) | Vendor-specific, not foundational | Skip (unless certification is the goal) |
| "SQL for beginners" books | Surface knowledge, no substance | Skip |
| Most "Big Data" books from the 2010s | Often overhyped surveys | Skip with caution |

### A note on contemporary relevance

Databases is in an unusually active period of contemporary development, with several substantial trends reshaping the field simultaneously.

The first major contemporary development is the cloud-native restructuring of database architecture. Traditional databases were designed for on-premises deployment with relatively static hardware; cloud-native databases are designed for dynamic resource allocation, geographic distribution, and elastic scaling. The architectures that have emerged — Snowflake's separation of compute and storage, the various serverless database offerings, the cloud-native variants of traditional databases — represent substantial departures from the pre-cloud architectures that the canonical textbooks describe. Understanding these developments requires engagement with recent papers and engineering blogs, since the textbook treatment lags by years.

The second development is the convergence of OLTP and OLAP workloads. The traditional separation between transactional databases (optimized for many small read-write operations) and analytical databases (optimized for large read-only queries over historical data) has been weakening, and a class of "hybrid" systems (HTAP — hybrid transactional/analytical processing) has emerged. The Snowflake, BigQuery, and various other cloud analytical systems represent one direction; the various NewSQL systems (CockroachDB, TiDB, Yugabyte) represent another; the analytical extensions to traditional OLTP databases represent a third. The architectural choices that distinguish these approaches are subtle and consequential.

The third development is the rise of streaming and real-time data systems. The traditional database model assumes that data is loaded into the database and then queried; streaming systems treat data as a continuous flow that is queried as it arrives. Apache Kafka, Apache Flink, the various other streaming platforms have established this paradigm, and modern data architectures often integrate streaming systems with traditional databases in ways that the canonical texts do not address. Engineering with this material requires engagement with recent literature.

The fourth development is the role of machine learning in database systems. There is active research on using machine learning to improve database components — learned indexes, learned cardinality estimation, learned query optimization, automatic tuning. The practical results have been mixed but interesting, and the subdiscipline is active enough that machine-learning-augmented databases are likely to become more common. Engineers serious about database internals should engage with this material.

The fifth development is the resurgence of specialized database systems. The general-purpose database, designed to handle all workloads adequately, is being supplemented by specialized systems designed for specific workloads (time-series databases, graph databases, vector databases, document databases). The recent rise of vector databases, driven by embeddings-based applications in machine learning, is the most visible recent example. The proliferation of specialized systems has costs (operational complexity, integration burden) and benefits (each system optimized for its specific workload), and the design choices in this space are actively being negotiated.

The sixth development, and the most uncertain, is the implications of generative AI for database use. LLMs can produce SQL queries from natural language descriptions, can suggest schema designs, can debug query performance problems. The implications for how databases are used in development and operation are substantial but not yet settled. Whether LLM-mediated database access becomes the dominant interaction pattern, or remains a supplement to direct database engagement, is one of the open questions about the field's near future.

Despite these developments, the foundational character of databases is robust. The relational model, the transaction concept, the basic techniques of indexing and query processing — these remain the foundation of database work, and contemporary developments build on them rather than replacing them. A learner who masters the foundational material is equipped to engage with whatever specific developments emerge, and the foundational material is what the canonical texts cover. The contemporary developments are valuable to know about and engage with, but they do not displace the need for foundational understanding.

## 2.8 — Databases

### What it is, properly

The conventional account of databases treats the subject as the study of systems for storing and retrieving structured data — a study organized around SQL, relational algebra, normalization, indexing, and the practical operation of database management systems. This account captures the surface of the subject without conveying what makes databases one of the deepest and most intellectually demanding subjects in computer science. A learner who completes a course taught at this level acquires the ability to write queries and design schemas without engaging with the conceptual content that justifies the subject's place in the foundational trunk.

A more honest account begins by recognizing that databases is the subject in which computer science has worked out, more thoroughly than anywhere else, what it means to *manage shared structured information reliably under concurrent access in the presence of failures*. Each clause of that description carries weight. *Shared* means that multiple users and processes interact with the same data simultaneously, and the system must coordinate among them. *Structured* means that the data has explicit schema, and the system enforces and exploits the structure. *Reliably* means that the data persists correctly across crashes, that updates either complete fully or appear not to have happened, and that the system's behavior is predictable under all conditions including failure. *Concurrent access* means that multiple operations proceed in parallel, and the system must produce results consistent with some serial execution while permitting parallelism for performance. *Failures* means that hardware fails, software crashes, networks partition, and the system must continue to function despite these failures.

The intellectual content of databases is the body of techniques developed over five decades for handling all of these concerns simultaneously, in a single coherent system, with provable guarantees about behavior. These techniques include: the relational model and its formal foundation in relational algebra and the relational calculus; the design of query languages and the optimization of queries; the construction of storage engines that maintain efficient access to data of arbitrary size; the implementation of transactions with the ACID properties; the various consistency models and isolation levels and their tradeoffs; the techniques for replication, partitioning, and distributed coordination; the recovery mechanisms that maintain durability across failures. These are not separable topics; they are the integrated content of a single subject, and the integration is what makes databases distinctive.

The reason this account matters is that databases is one of the few subjects in computer science where engineering practice and theoretical foundation have been worked out together, with each constraining and informing the other. The relational model is a formal mathematical theory that also produces practical systems; the ACID properties are precise technical guarantees that also serve as engineering specifications; query optimization is an algorithmic discipline that also operates in production systems handling billions of queries per day. The unity of theory and practice in databases is unusual in computer science, and engaging with the subject seriously means engaging with both sides simultaneously.

The content of the subject, examined seriously, is structured around four layers of concern. The first layer is the *data model*: the formal account of what data is and how it is structured. The relational model is the dominant data model, but other models (hierarchical, network, document, graph, key-value) coexist with it and each has its place. The second layer is the *query language and processing*: the means by which users and applications interact with the data, expressed declaratively (in SQL or its alternatives) and executed by an engine that translates queries into efficient execution plans. The third layer is the *storage and indexing*: the on-disk and in-memory structures that hold the data and support efficient access. This is where database systems engage most deeply with operating-systems and architectural concerns, and where most of the engineering complexity of database systems lives. The fourth layer is the *transaction and concurrency management*: the mechanisms that allow multiple operations to proceed concurrently while maintaining the database's invariants, and that allow operations to recover correctly from failures.

### The deep problems the field tries to answer

Four problems organize databases as a discipline.

**The problem of declarative data access.** The user of a database wants to specify *what* data is desired, not *how* to retrieve it. The system, in turn, must figure out the most efficient way to retrieve that data given the current state of the database (its size, its indexing, its statistical properties, the system's current load). The separation between what (declarative specification) and how (procedural execution) is one of the most consequential abstractions in computer science: it allows the same query to be optimized differently for different database states, allows the database to evolve without requiring application code to change, and allows the optimization techniques to improve over time without disturbing the queries that use them. The achievement is the recognition that data access can be specified at a high abstraction level and that the gap to efficient execution can be bridged systematically by the database system. The mechanisms — query optimization, statistical estimation, plan caching, adaptive execution — constitute a substantial body of intellectual work and are continually being refined.

**The problem of transactional correctness.** A transaction is a unit of work that must execute atomically (it either completes entirely or appears not to have happened), consistently (it preserves the database's invariants), in isolation (its intermediate states are not visible to other transactions), and durably (its effects survive system failures). These four properties — the ACID properties — sound straightforward but are remarkably difficult to provide simultaneously, particularly in the presence of concurrent transactions and system failures. The mechanisms that provide them — write-ahead logging, multi-version concurrency control, two-phase locking, the various forms of snapshot isolation — are conceptually deep and have been refined over decades. The intellectual achievement is the recognition that complex multi-step operations can be made to appear atomic at the application level, that the appearance of atomicity can survive arbitrary failure scenarios, and that the cost of providing this appearance is bounded and predictable.

**The problem of efficient storage and access.** A database may contain terabytes or petabytes of data, far more than fits in memory, and yet must support fast access to arbitrary records. The mechanisms — B-trees and their variants, log-structured merge trees, columnar storage, compression, partitioning, indexing — have been refined over fifty years and continue to be developed. Each storage technique trades off different concerns: insertion speed against query speed, space efficiency against access efficiency, write amplification against read amplification, point-query performance against analytical query performance. The intellectual achievement is the recognition that storage and indexing decisions are not independent of workload, that different workloads require different storage techniques, and that database systems can be designed to use different storage techniques for different parts of their data.

**The problem of distributed data management.** When data is too large for a single machine, or when applications require higher availability than a single machine can provide, data must be distributed across multiple machines. Distribution introduces problems that do not exist in single-machine databases: how to maintain consistency across replicas, how to coordinate transactions that span multiple machines, how to handle partial failures where some machines are available and others are not, how to scale operations that involve data across the distribution. The CAP theorem and its successors (the PACELC theorem, the various impossibility results in distributed systems) establish fundamental limits on what distributed databases can provide. The mechanisms — consensus protocols, quorum reads and writes, conflict-free replicated data types, the various consistency models — are technically intricate and represent some of the most active research in the field. The intellectual achievement is the recognition that distribution introduces fundamental tradeoffs that no system can avoid, and that the right design choice depends on the specific requirements of the application.

### Where it sits in the map

Databases occupies a central position in the foundational trunk because it is the subject in which the data axis of computer science is most fully developed. It depends on operating systems (Section 2.5) for storage management, file systems, and concurrency primitives. It depends on computer networks (Section 2.6) for distributed database operation. It depends on algorithms and data structures (Section 2.7) for the substantial algorithmic content of query processing, indexing, and concurrency control. It depends on the theory of computation (Section 2.3) for the formal foundations of the relational model and for complexity-theoretic results on query evaluation.

The relationship to distributed systems (Section 2.9) is particularly close. Distributed databases is one of the major subareas of distributed systems, and many of the most important results in distributed systems were originally developed in the context of database research (two-phase commit, the ARIES recovery protocol, the various consistency models, the early work on replication). The boundary between distributed databases and distributed systems generally is not sharp, and serious treatments of either substantially engage with the other.

The relationship to programming is one in which databases extends what programs can do. Most non-trivial programs interact with databases — to store user data, to retain state across executions, to support concurrent access by multiple users, to implement transactions. The discipline of programming with databases involves understanding what databases can and cannot do, what guarantees they provide, what their performance characteristics are, and how to design applications that work well with database systems. Programmers without database training often misuse databases — using them as simple key-value stores, ignoring the ACID guarantees, failing to use indexes appropriately, treating them as bottlenecks rather than as engineered systems with predictable performance — and the cost of these errors is substantial in production systems.

The relationship to specific application areas is generally that databases provides the data-management substrate while the application areas provide the specific data structures and access patterns. Web applications use databases for user data and session state. Analytics systems use databases (often with specialized engines) for query-intensive workloads. Machine-learning systems use databases for training-data management and feature storage. Financial systems use databases for transaction processing under stringent correctness requirements. Each application area has its own specialized concerns, but the foundation is the same: the techniques developed in databases are the conceptual basis for the data-management work that follows.

The position within the four axes of Chapter 1 is squarely on the data axis, with substantial engagement on the system axis (because databases are large engineered systems with substantial systems-engineering content) and on the computation axis (because query processing involves substantial algorithmic content). The intelligence axis is engaged increasingly through the use of databases in machine-learning workflows and through recent work on learned database components.

### What you become after studying it

A reader who has internalized databases acquires several capacities that distinguish them from programmers who treat databases as opaque storage.

The first is *fluency in the relational model and its query language*. The trained practitioner thinks in relational terms when designing data structures, recognizes when a problem has relational structure, and can express complex queries in SQL or its equivalents with facility. The relational model is one of the most consequential intellectual achievements of computer science, and the fluency it produces is a permanent gain that transfers across database systems and across decades. Practitioners who lack this fluency often try to express relational queries through ad-hoc procedural code, producing solutions that are slower, less reliable, and harder to maintain than the corresponding declarative queries.

The second capacity is *understanding of transactional semantics*. The trained practitioner knows what ACID properties guarantee, what the various isolation levels mean precisely, and how to reason about concurrent operations on shared data. This understanding is essential for any work that involves shared mutable state, which is to say, most non-trivial software. The practitioner can read application code that uses a database and recognize whether the application's correctness depends on properties the database actually provides; the untrained practitioner cannot make this judgment and is therefore unable to verify that the application is correct.

The third capacity is *the ability to reason about query performance*. Database query performance is determined by the interaction between the query, the data, the schema, the indexes, and the optimizer's choices. The trained practitioner can read a query and estimate how it will perform, can identify when a query is unlikely to scale, and can make schema and indexing decisions that improve performance predictably. This skill is acquired through experience with database systems and is one of the major returns on database study. Practitioners who lack it often write queries that work on small datasets and fail catastrophically on production-scale data, with the failure attributed to "database problems" rather than to the engineer's choices.

The fourth capacity is *the ability to choose appropriate database technology*. The contemporary database landscape is fragmented: relational databases, document databases, graph databases, time-series databases, search databases, key-value stores, distributed analytical databases, and many others, each with characteristic strengths and limitations. The trained practitioner can match a database technology to a workload — knowing when a relational database is the right choice and when an alternative is preferable, knowing what the tradeoffs are, knowing how to evaluate vendors' claims. This judgment is acquired through engagement with the database literature and with multiple database systems, and it is increasingly important as the database landscape continues to fragment.

The fifth capacity is *appreciation for the depth of database systems*. Database management systems are among the largest and most sophisticated software systems in existence, with codebases that have been refined over decades and that incorporate techniques drawn from across computer science. The trained practitioner has some sense of what these systems are doing internally, why they are so complex, and what the tradeoffs in their design are. This appreciation is what allows the practitioner to use database systems effectively and to recognize when problems they encounter are problems with the database versus problems with their use of it.

### How the field sees the world

Databases embeds a worldview that is unusually distinct among CS subjects, and that has shaped how serious data management is conducted across the field.

The first commitment is *to the centrality of structure*. The database worldview treats data as fundamentally structured: it has schema, the schema is enforced, and the structure is exploited for both correctness and efficiency. This commitment runs against the contemporary fashion for "schemaless" systems, which treat structure as an inconvenience to be deferred or avoided. The database worldview holds that schema is not a constraint to be minimized but a tool to be used: schema enables the database to enforce invariants, to optimize storage and access, to support declarative queries, and to evolve the data model in controlled ways. Systems that abandon schema typically reproduce schema enforcement at the application level, often inconsistently and unreliably, and the abandonment is rarely the savings it appears to be. The mature database practitioner takes schema seriously as an engineering tool, designs schemas carefully, and treats schema evolution as a first-class concern.

The second commitment is *to the priority of correctness*. The ACID properties are not optional features; they are the foundation on which application correctness is built. The database worldview holds that systems which provide weaker guarantees (eventually consistent, "best-effort" durability, etc.) are appropriate for specific workloads but are not general substitutes for systems that provide ACID guarantees. The contemporary movement toward eventual consistency, in particular, is regarded with suspicion by mature database practitioners: the consistency guarantees that eventually consistent systems provide are often weaker than applications need, and the burden of reasoning about consistency is shifted to application developers who are typically less equipped to handle it than database systems are. This is not a categorical rejection of weaker consistency models — they are appropriate when their tradeoffs match the application's needs — but it is a rejection of treating weaker consistency as a default. The mature practitioner uses the strongest consistency that the system requires and weakens it only with explicit justification.

The third commitment is *to the value of declarative specification*. The relational model and its query languages are declarative: users specify what data they want, and the system determines how to retrieve it. The database worldview holds that this separation is one of the most productive abstractions in computer science, because it allows the same query to be optimized differently as the database changes, because it makes queries portable across implementations, and because it allows the optimization techniques to improve over time. The contemporary movement toward procedural data access (through ORMs, through ad-hoc code, through "NoSQL" APIs that expose imperative operations) is, from the database perspective, a regression: it sacrifices the declarative abstraction that makes database systems powerful in exchange for short-term programmer convenience. The mature practitioner uses declarative queries when possible and falls back to procedural code only when the declarative approach genuinely cannot express the required computation.

The fourth commitment is *to the durability of data*. The database worldview takes for granted that data, once committed, must survive any failure of the system: power loss, hardware failure, software crash, operator error. The mechanisms for ensuring this — write-ahead logging, fsync discipline, replication, backup — are not optional features but are foundational to what databases are. This commitment shapes how database systems are designed and operated, and how applications interact with them. Applications that depend on data durability and use systems that do not actually provide it are common, and the resulting data losses are often catastrophic when they occur. The mature practitioner verifies that the systems they depend on actually provide the durability they require, treating vendor claims with appropriate skepticism and testing failure scenarios deliberately.

These four commitments — to structure, to correctness, to declarative specification, and to durability — distinguish the database worldview from adjacent worldviews in software engineering. Practitioners who have absorbed them treat data as a serious engineering concern with its own discipline; practitioners who have not often treat data management as an afterthought, with predictable consequences for the systems they build.

### Common pitfalls in learning it

The pitfalls of learning databases are characteristic and recurring, and the most damaging are particular to the subject's combination of theoretical foundation, engineering complexity, and ubiquitous practical use.

The most common pitfall is *the SQL-only view*. Many learners encounter databases primarily through SQL — they learn to write queries, design schemas, and use database systems through their query interfaces, without engaging with what the database is doing internally. SQL fluency is necessary, and it is the right starting point, but it is not the substance of databases. A learner who knows SQL well but has not studied query optimization, indexing, transaction management, and storage internals has not studied databases — they have studied the user-facing surface of databases, which is a much smaller subject. The countermeasure is to recognize SQL as the entry point rather than the destination, and to engage seriously with the internal mechanisms that make SQL work.

A second pitfall is *the ORM trap*. Object-relational mappers (ORMs) are libraries that present database functionality through object-oriented programming interfaces, allowing developers to interact with databases without writing SQL directly. ORMs are productively used in many contexts, but they have substantial costs: they often produce inefficient queries, they hide the database's actual behavior from the developer, and they encourage developers to think of databases as object stores rather than as relational systems. Developers who learn database access primarily through ORMs often acquire confused understandings of what the database is doing, and they struggle when ORM-mediated access does not produce acceptable performance. The countermeasure is to learn raw SQL and the underlying database concepts before adopting ORMs, and to maintain the habit of reading the SQL that ORMs generate to verify that it is reasonable.

A third pitfall is *the NoSQL confusion*. The NoSQL movement of the 2000s and 2010s produced a substantial number of database systems that abandoned various properties of traditional relational databases (schema enforcement, ACID transactions, SQL queries) in exchange for various other properties (horizontal scalability, flexible data models, simpler operational characteristics). The movement was useful in expanding the database design space, but it also produced confusion about what the tradeoffs actually are. Many learners absorbed marketing claims about NoSQL systems being "more scalable" or "more flexible" than relational databases without understanding the specific senses in which these claims are or are not true. The countermeasure is to engage with NoSQL systems as instances of specific design choices in the broader database design space, rather than as alternatives to a single thing called "SQL." The mature practitioner can articulate precisely what each NoSQL system gives up and what it gains, and can match systems to workloads accordingly.

A fourth pitfall is *neglecting transaction theory*. Transactions are conceptually difficult, and their treatment in introductory texts is often perfunctory: the ACID properties are listed, the standard isolation levels are described, and the discussion moves on. The deeper material — the various concurrency control techniques, the formal models of consistency, the recovery algorithms — is often relegated to advanced courses. Learners who skip this material end up with shallow understandings of what databases actually guarantee and how. The countermeasure is to allocate substantial study time to transaction theory and to engage with the primary literature, particularly the papers on multi-version concurrency control, snapshot isolation, and the various formal consistency models.

A fifth pitfall is *the assumption that relational databases are obsolete*. Periodically, the database community is told that relational databases are being superseded by some new technology — object databases in the 1990s, XML databases in the 2000s, NoSQL in the 2010s, NewSQL more recently. Each wave has produced useful systems and useful ideas, but none has actually superseded relational databases, which remain the dominant data-management technology by a wide margin. Learners who believe the supersession claims often invest in technologies that turn out to be less central than expected, and underinvest in the relational foundation that remains essential. The countermeasure is to take the long view: relational databases have been dominant for forty years, the techniques are deeply refined, and they are likely to remain central for decades. New technologies are worth engaging with on their merits, but not as replacements for the relational foundation.

A sixth pitfall is *the operational neglect*. Database systems are operationally complex: they require careful configuration, monitoring, backup, and tuning to function well in production. Academic treatments often ignore this operational dimension entirely, focusing on the conceptual content. Learners who acquire only the conceptual content are surprised when they encounter the operational realities of running databases in production, and they often make decisions that seem reasonable conceptually but produce operational disasters. The countermeasure is to supplement conceptual study with engagement with the operational literature: the various administration guides for major databases, the postmortems of database-related production incidents, the practitioner-oriented blogs that discuss database operations.

A seventh pitfall is *the cloud-database illusion*. Cloud database services (Amazon RDS, Google Cloud SQL, Azure Database, the various managed database offerings) abstract away substantial portions of database operation, and learners who use these services may conclude that database operation is no longer a concern. This conclusion is partially correct: cloud services do handle many of the operational concerns that on-premises database operation involves. It is also partially wrong: cloud database services have their own operational characteristics, their own failure modes, their own tradeoffs, and serious cloud database work requires understanding both the underlying database technology and the specific cloud service's behavior. The countermeasure is to recognize that cloud services abstract operation but do not eliminate database concerns, and to maintain the underlying database competence that allows the abstractions to be used effectively.

### The literature of the subject

Databases has an unusually well-organized literature, with stable canonical texts and a rich ecosystem of contemporary sources. The selections below cover the standard treatments and the supplementary materials that contemporary practice requires.

#### Canonical entry points

The most widely used contemporary entry to databases is Garcia-Molina, Ullman, and Widom's *Database Systems: The Complete Book*. The book covers the full breadth of database systems — data models, query languages, query processing, storage, transactions, distributed databases — at a level appropriate for a serious first course. Its distinctive feature is its balance of theoretical foundation and practical content: the relational model is treated with the formal care it deserves, and the implementation techniques are covered at sufficient depth to support understanding of how database systems actually work. For self-study, Garcia-Molina is among the best options.

The classical alternative is Silberschatz, Korth, and Sudarshan's *Database System Concepts*. The book is comprehensive and well-organized, with clear pedagogy and substantial examples. It is more conservative than Garcia-Molina in its coverage of newer topics (NoSQL, distributed databases) but covers the traditional content with great care. For learners who prefer a more traditional textbook approach, Silberschatz is the right choice.

The deeper-than-introductory standard reference is Hellerstein and Stonebraker's *Architecture of a Database System*, available freely as a chapter in *Readings in Database Systems*. This is not a textbook but a survey paper, and it provides the most concise and useful introduction to how database systems are actually structured internally. Reading it after the introductory texts is valuable: it consolidates the introductory material and provides the architectural framework that detailed implementation work depends on.

The reading order recommendation: Garcia-Molina (or Silberschatz) as the primary introductory text, with Hellerstein-Stonebraker read after the introductory text to consolidate the architectural picture. The course materials from Berkeley's CS 186 (taught by Hellerstein for many years and freely available online) and from CMU's database systems courses (taught by Andy Pavlo and freely available, with exceptional video lectures) are excellent companions and substantially extend what reading alone provides.

#### The progression

After the canonical introduction, the progression in databases branches according to specialization.

*Database internals at depth.* The standard text for serious engagement with database internals is Hellerstein and Stonebraker's *Readings in Database Systems* (the "Red Book"), an anthology of foundational papers in databases that has been maintained and updated over five editions. The fifth edition (2015), edited by Bailis, Hellerstein, and Stonebraker, is the current standard. Reading the Red Book systematically, working through the papers in each section in the order indicated, is one of the most effective ways to acquire deep database knowledge. The papers themselves are foundational works of the field, and engagement with them gives a sense of how the major ideas in databases were developed.

*Storage engines.* Modern database storage engines are substantial engineering artifacts, and several books cover them at depth. Petrov's *Database Internals* is a contemporary text that covers storage engine implementation with substantial detail, including the modern log-structured merge tree designs that underlie many contemporary databases. The book is well-paced and accessible to learners with introductory database preparation.

*Transaction processing.* The classical reference is Gray and Reuter's *Transaction Processing: Concepts and Techniques*, a comprehensive treatment of transaction processing from one of the field's foundational figures. The book is dated in some specifics but remains the deepest single treatment of transactions available, and it is the right reference for any serious work on transaction processing. Bernstein, Hadzilacos, and Goodman's *Concurrency Control and Recovery in Database Systems* is the more focused alternative for the specific topics of concurrency control and recovery, and is freely available online.

*Query processing.* The standard treatment of query processing at depth is Graefe's "Query Evaluation Techniques for Large Databases" (a long survey paper from 1993, freely available). Graefe's paper is substantially more thorough than textbook treatments and is the right reference for serious work on query processing. The various papers on contemporary query processing (vectorized execution, just-in-time compilation, adaptive query processing) extend the picture into contemporary practice.

*Distributed databases.* Kleppmann's *Designing Data-Intensive Applications* is the contemporary standard for engineers who need to understand distributed databases in practice. The book covers the major techniques for distributed data management — replication, partitioning, transactions in distributed systems, the various consistency models — at a level appropriate for working engineers. Kleppmann is widely regarded as one of the best contemporary database books, and it has become a standard reference for engineers who work with modern distributed data systems.

*Specific database systems.* For engineers who work primarily with specific systems, the documentation and internals references for those systems are essential. PostgreSQL's documentation is unusually comprehensive and well-written, and is appropriate as primary reading for engineers working with PostgreSQL. The various books on specific systems (the *MySQL Internals* book, *PostgreSQL Internals*, the Cassandra and MongoDB references) are appropriate as system-specific deep dives after the foundational material is in place.

#### Reference works

*Database Systems: The Complete Book* and *Database System Concepts*, mentioned above as primary texts, are also reference works. Both are sufficiently comprehensive that working database engineers consult them when they need to look up specific topics.

The *Readings in Database Systems* anthology (the Red Book), mentioned above, is a continuing reference for the field's foundational papers. Engineers consult it when they need to engage with the original literature on a specific topic.

The PostgreSQL and SQLite source code and documentation are reference works in their own right. PostgreSQL is widely regarded as the best-engineered open-source database, and its source code is unusually readable for a system of its complexity. Engineers who want to understand how production database systems are actually implemented can read PostgreSQL's source profitably. SQLite is much smaller (roughly 150,000 lines of code) and is therefore more approachable for full reading; its source is famously well-tested and well-documented.

The various SQL standards documents (SQL:1992, SQL:1999, SQL:2003, and successors) are technical references for SQL itself. Most working database engineers do not consult these directly — the documentation of specific database systems is usually more useful — but they are the authoritative source for what SQL is supposed to do.

#### Heterodox correctives

Codd's original papers on the relational model — particularly "A Relational Model of Data for Large Shared Data Banks" (1970) — are foundational reading. Reading Codd directly, after introductory study, is illuminating: the paper is unusually clear, the arguments are made carefully, and the historical context (the paper was a polemic against the dominant network and hierarchical data models of its time) is visible in the writing. The paper is short and accessible, and it represents one of the founding intellectual achievements of computer science.

Stonebraker's various papers and writings constitute a heterodox corrective in the sense that Stonebraker has been one of the most consistently outspoken critics of various database orthodoxies over forty years. His papers on the limitations of the relational model for specific workloads, on the failures of the NoSQL movement, on the architectural choices in modern database systems, are valuable reading regardless of whether one agrees with his conclusions. The collection of his papers and the various interviews he has given are accessible online.

Date's writings on the relational model — particularly *An Introduction to Database Systems* (now in its eighth edition) and *SQL and Relational Theory* — are heterodox in their insistence on the formal relational model. Date is among the most rigorous defenders of the relational model and is sharply critical of SQL's deviations from the relational ideal. Reading Date is illuminating for understanding what the relational model actually is, as distinct from what SQL implements; the gap between the two is larger than most learners realize.

Hellerstein's various writings on the limits of conventional database systems — particularly his work on the CALM theorem and on coordination-free distributed systems — are heterodox in their arguments that fundamental rethinking of database architecture is needed for contemporary workloads. The papers are dense but accessible, and they represent some of the most interesting contemporary thinking about where databases are going.

#### What to skip and why

Most introductory database textbooks not mentioned above can be skipped if the canonical texts are available. The genre is saturated, and minor variants offer little.

Books that focus exclusively on specific database systems (the various "Learning MySQL" or "PostgreSQL Cookbook" books) are appropriate as system-specific references but should not be primary learning material. They tend to teach the system's user interface without engaging with the underlying database concepts.

Books on database administration that focus on operational tasks (backup procedures, configuration parameters, monitoring) are appropriate for engineers working in database administration but should not be confused with database study. The operational material is valuable but is downstream of the conceptual material that the foundational texts cover.

Most "NoSQL" books published during the 2010s are dated and partially misleading. The NoSQL movement has matured, and many of the early claims for specific NoSQL systems have not been borne out by subsequent experience. Engineers who want to understand contemporary NoSQL systems should engage with current documentation and current papers rather than with the early polemical literature.

Books that promise to teach databases through a single technology (the various "Build a database in Rust" tutorials, for example) should be approached with caution. Some are genuinely educational; many are shallow despite technical competence. The canonical texts produce more reliable database understanding than language-specific tutorials.

Most online database tutorials are uneven in quality. The exceptions — the various deep technical posts from database vendors, the Use the Index Luke website on database indexing, Markus Winand's writings — are valuable, but the typical tutorial is too shallow to be worth the time investment compared to canonical texts.

#### Contemporary sources

Databases is an active research field with a rich contemporary ecosystem.

The major database conferences are SIGMOD (the ACM database conference), VLDB (the international database conference), ICDE (data engineering), and CIDR (the more research-focused alternative venue). Their proceedings represent the research frontier of the field, and many papers are accessible to readers with introductory preparation.

Several blogs and online resources are valuable for contemporary engagement. Andy Pavlo's *Database of Databases* (db-engines.com is the parallel resource maintained by others) catalogues contemporary database systems and is useful for understanding the landscape. The CMU Database Group's YouTube channel contains exceptional lectures on database internals and contemporary research. The various vendor blogs — Google's papers and blog posts on Spanner and BigQuery, Amazon's writings on DynamoDB and Aurora, the various PostgreSQL contributors' writings — provide insight into how production database systems are actually designed and operated.

The "Use The Index, Luke!" website by Markus Winand is the standard resource for understanding database indexing in practice, and it is one of the few high-quality online resources on a specific database topic.

Database-focused practitioners' writings — Kyle Kingsbury's *Jepsen* analyses of distributed database correctness, Henrik Ingo's writings on database internals, Peter Bailis's papers and posts on consistency — are valuable for staying current with how the field is actually developing.

The various papers on contemporary database research — the work on learned indexes, the work on disaggregated database architectures, the work on serverless databases, the work on databases for machine learning — are at the research frontier and are appropriate for readers who want to understand where the field is heading.

#### Reference table

| Resource | Role | Tag |
|---|---|---|
