### A note on contemporary relevance

Databases is in an interesting contemporary position. The relational core has been remarkably stable — the major relational databases (PostgreSQL, MySQL, Oracle, SQL Server, DB2) have been refined over decades and continue to dominate most workloads. But the periphery has expanded substantially, and several developments are reshaping the broader database landscape.

The most consequential contemporary development is the maturation of distributed databases. The CAP theorem and the various impossibility results from distributed systems established the limits of what distributed databases can provide, but the engineering of systems that operate well within these limits has advanced substantially. Google's Spanner demonstrated that geo-distributed strongly-consistent transactions are possible at scale, contradicting earlier consensus that they were not. The CockroachDB, YugabyteDB, and TiDB systems have brought Spanner-like capabilities to the broader market. The various managed cloud database services (AWS Aurora, Google Cloud Spanner, Azure Cosmos DB) have made sophisticated distributed databases broadly accessible. The implications for database architecture are still being worked out, and the field is in the middle of substantial rethinking of what distributed databases can be.

The second major development is the rise of analytical databases for large-scale data analysis. Systems like Snowflake, BigQuery, Redshift, and Databricks have established the cloud data warehouse as a distinct category of database system, optimized for analytical queries on large datasets rather than for transactional workloads. The architectural choices in these systems — separation of storage from compute, columnar storage, vectorized execution, massive parallelism — represent a substantial departure from traditional database architecture, and the systems have been commercially very successful. The implications for the broader database field are substantial: analytical workloads are increasingly handled by specialized systems rather than by general-purpose databases, and the boundary between transactional and analytical processing is increasingly porous.

The third development is the emergence of streaming databases and the convergence of databases with stream processing. Systems like Apache Kafka, Apache Flink, and Materialize have established that some workloads are better served by treating data as streams of events rather than as static collections of records. The streaming approach has implications for how databases are designed, how applications interact with them, and how data architectures are organized. The convergence of databases and stream processing is one of the more interesting contemporary developments, and the resulting systems do not fit neatly into traditional database taxonomies.

The fourth development is the role of machine learning in databases. There is active research on using machine learning to improve standard database functions (learned indexes, learned query optimization, learned cache replacement, automatic database tuning), and there is active research on databases specifically designed to support machine-learning workloads (feature stores, vector databases, the various specialized systems for storing and serving machine-learning model data). The practical results so far are mixed but interesting, and the subdiscipline is sufficiently active that machine-learning-augmented databases are likely to become increasingly common.

The fifth development is the resurgence of interest in database fundamentals through the open-source database ecosystem. PostgreSQL has, over the past decade, established itself as the dominant open-source relational database and has continued to evolve substantially: extensions for time-series data, for full-text search, for spatial data, and for many other workloads have made PostgreSQL into a single platform that handles workloads previously requiring specialized systems. The "PostgreSQL for everything" movement is a reaction against the fragmentation of the database landscape, and it represents a serious bet that a single high-quality database system can handle most workloads with appropriate extensions.

Despite these contemporary developments, the foundational character of databases is robust. The relational model still dominates. SQL is still the standard query language. The ACID properties are still the foundation of reliable data management. The classical techniques — query optimization, indexing, transactions, recovery — are still the substance of database systems. A learner who masters the foundational material is equipped to engage with contemporary developments, and the foundational material is what the canonical texts cover. The contemporary developments add to the foundational picture but do not replace it.

## 2.9 — Distributed Systems

### What it is, properly

The conventional account of distributed systems treats the subject as the engineering of systems composed of multiple computers communicating over networks — a study organized around specific technologies (RPC frameworks, distributed databases, message queues, cluster managers) and specific concerns (latency, throughput, availability). This account is partially accurate but substantially understates what makes distributed systems one of the most intellectually demanding subjects in computer science. A learner who completes a course at this level acquires familiarity with vocabulary and architectures without engaging with the conceptual content that justifies the subject's central position in contemporary computing.

A more honest account begins by recognizing that distributed systems is the subject in which the assumptions of single-machine computation are systematically abandoned, and in which the conceptual framework for thinking about computation across independent failure-prone communicating processes is established. Single-machine computation assumes shared memory accessible at uniform cost, a single global clock, atomic operations on shared state, and predictable failure modes (when the machine fails, all of it fails together). Distributed systems removes every one of these assumptions: communication is unreliable and asynchronous, there is no shared memory and no global clock, atomic operations across machines are difficult and expensive, and failures are partial — some machines fail while others continue operating, often without those still operating being able to determine which is which. The intellectual content of distributed systems is the body of techniques, theorems, and conceptual frameworks developed for building reliable computation in this fundamentally more difficult setting.

The subject contains some of the deepest results in computer science: the FLP impossibility result on consensus in asynchronous systems with even a single failure, the CAP theorem and its successors on the tradeoffs between consistency and availability under network partitions, the various lower bounds on the message complexity and latency of distributed algorithms, the impossibility results on coordination without sufficient information. These results are genuine theorems about what distributed computation can and cannot achieve, and they shape what serious distributed systems work looks like. They are not engineering observations; they are mathematical impossibilities, established by proof, that no amount of cleverness can circumvent. The intellectual achievement of the field has been the recognition that distributed computation is fundamentally different from centralized computation in well-defined ways, and the development of techniques that work productively within the established limits.

The content of the subject, examined seriously, is structured around several layers of conceptual difficulty. At the lowest layer is the *system model*: the formal account of what a distributed system is, what assumptions can be made about its components, and what failures can occur. The choice of system model is itself a substantial intellectual concern: synchronous models (where messages have bounded delivery times) admit techniques that asynchronous models (where messages may take arbitrarily long) do not, and the gap between what is possible in different models is one of the central concerns of the field. At the middle layer are the *fundamental problems*: consensus (getting multiple machines to agree on a value), atomic broadcast (delivering messages to multiple recipients in the same order), leader election (designating one machine as authoritative), distributed mutual exclusion (coordinating access to shared resources), distributed snapshots (capturing consistent global state), and the various replication problems. At the highest layer are the *systems and architectures*: the actual designs of distributed systems that solve practical problems by composing solutions to the fundamental problems. This is where the contemporary work most visible to engineers happens — the design of distributed databases, distributed file systems, microservice architectures, blockchain systems, and so on.

The reason this account matters is that distributed systems, more than any other CS subject, rewards the practitioner who has internalized its conceptual framework and punishes the practitioner who has not. The number of ways to build a distributed system that appears to work and has subtle correctness bugs is enormous, and the bugs typically emerge only under conditions that are difficult to reproduce — specific patterns of message loss, specific timing of concurrent operations, specific failure scenarios. Engineers who lack the conceptual framework build systems that work in testing and fail in production, often with consequences that are difficult to diagnose and expensive to remedy. Engineers who have the framework recognize when their designs are crossing into difficult territory and either avoid the difficulty or address it with appropriate care. The difference between these two modes of practice is one of the largest gaps in contemporary engineering competence, and it is established almost entirely by whether and how seriously the engineer has studied distributed systems.

### The deep problems the field tries to answer

Four problems organize distributed systems as a discipline.

**The problem of agreement under uncertainty.** Multiple machines need to agree on a value — what the next state of the system should be, who holds a lock, which transaction commits — under conditions where communication is unreliable, machines can fail, and no machine has complete information about the others. The naive approach of having one machine declare the value and others accept it fails under failures: if the declaring machine crashes after some recipients have learned the value but before others have, the system is in an inconsistent state with no obvious recovery. The actual approach requires protocols in which agreement emerges from coordinated exchange of information, with the protocol providing guarantees about the conditions under which agreement is reached. The mechanisms — Paxos, Raft, Viewstamped Replication, Byzantine fault-tolerant consensus, and many variants — are technically intricate and have been refined over four decades. The intellectual achievement is the recognition that agreement is genuinely difficult under these conditions, that the difficulty is fundamental rather than incidental, and that protocols can be designed that operate correctly within the constraints. The FLP impossibility result establishes that no asynchronous protocol can guarantee both safety and liveness in the presence of even one failure, and the practical protocols all relax one of these properties (typically liveness, allowing progress only when the system stabilizes) to achieve the other.

**The problem of consistent shared state.** Multiple machines hold copies or partitions of shared state — data, configuration, application state — and operations on this state must produce consistent results. The simplest consistency model — strong consistency, where all machines see the same state at the same time — is impossible to provide without sacrificing availability under network partitions, as established by the CAP theorem. The actual landscape of consistency models is more nuanced: linearizability (operations appear to take effect at some single point in time), sequential consistency (operations appear to occur in some sequential order consistent with each process's order), causal consistency (operations consistent with their causal relationships), eventual consistency (replicas converge if updates stop), and many others. Each model represents a different tradeoff among consistency, availability, and performance, and choosing the right model for a workload is one of the central design decisions in distributed systems. The mechanisms for implementing each model — quorum protocols, vector clocks, conflict-free replicated data types, the various replication protocols — are individually deep and collectively comprise a substantial body of the field's intellectual content.

**The problem of fault tolerance.** Distributed systems are built from components that fail, and the system as a whole must continue functioning despite component failures. The naive approach of preventing failures fails: at sufficient scale, failures are continuous and unavoidable, and any system whose correctness depends on failures not occurring will not function. The actual approach is to design systems that *expect* failures and handle them as routine events rather than as exceptions. The mechanisms — replication, redundancy, retry with idempotency, graceful degradation, circuit breakers, the various failure detection techniques — are each individually well-defined, but the conceptual achievement is the recognition that fault tolerance is a property of the system architecture rather than a feature to be added. Systems designed without fault tolerance from the beginning typically cannot be retrofitted to be fault-tolerant; the architecture itself must be designed to handle failures, and this design must occur at the start rather than as an afterthought.

**The problem of scaling beyond a single machine.** Single machines have bounded capacity — bounded CPU, bounded memory, bounded storage, bounded network bandwidth. Many workloads exceed what a single machine can handle, and the natural response is to distribute the work across multiple machines. The naive approach — taking a single-machine system and replicating it — usually does not scale, because the scaling bottlenecks (shared state, coordination overhead, the various forms of contention) exist within the system itself rather than within the machine. The actual approach requires designing systems whose architecture admits horizontal scaling: data is partitioned so that operations on different partitions do not coordinate, state is kept local where possible, coordination is minimized and isolated to specific points. The mechanisms — sharding, the various forms of partitioning, eventual consistency where strong consistency is not required, the careful management of stateful versus stateless components — are individually well-understood but collectively require sustained attention to the architecture of the system. Systems that achieve horizontal scaling do so by design, not by accident, and the design discipline required is one of the major intellectual contributions of the field.

### Where it sits in the map

Distributed systems sits at a unique position in the foundational trunk: it is the most demanding of the trunk subjects, it depends substantially on most of the others, and it is the subject in which the trunk most fully engages with contemporary practice.

The relationship to operating systems (Section 2.5) is generalization. Distributed systems extends the resource-arbitration framework that operating systems develops from a single machine to a collection of machines. Many of the conceptual techniques are similar — virtualization, isolation, transactional state — but the techniques must be reimplemented across machines, where shared memory and synchronous primitives are not available. A practitioner who has studied operating systems has the conceptual foundation for distributed systems; a practitioner who has not will struggle to acquire both subjects simultaneously.

The relationship to computer networks (Section 2.6) is layering. Distributed systems builds on the communication primitives that networks provides and addresses the higher-level coordination problems that arise once communication is given. The two subjects are sometimes presented together, and there are intellectual arguments for the combined presentation. The decision to treat them separately reflects a pedagogical judgment: the layered relationship makes networks the appropriate prerequisite for distributed systems, and engaging with distributed systems before having the networks foundation tends to produce confusion about what is a network concern and what is a coordination concern.

The relationship to databases (Section 2.8) is overlap. Distributed databases is one of the major subareas of distributed systems, and many of the most important results in distributed systems were originally developed in the context of database research. The boundary between distributed databases and distributed systems generally is not sharp, and serious treatments of either substantially engage with the other. A learner who has studied databases has substantial preparation for distributed systems, and vice versa, but neither subject is a substitute for the other.

The relationship to algorithms (Section 2.7) is that distributed algorithms are a substantial subdiscipline of algorithms, with their own techniques, their own analytical framework, and their own canonical problems. The complexity measures relevant to distributed algorithms (message complexity, round complexity, time complexity in synchronous models) differ from the complexity measures relevant to sequential algorithms, and the techniques (the various consensus protocols, the broadcast protocols, the snapshot protocols) are specific to the distributed setting. Familiarity with sequential algorithms is necessary preparation, but distributed algorithms is largely a separate body of material.

The relationship to programming is one in which distributed systems extends what programs can do but also constrains how they must be written. Programs that interact with distributed systems must handle partial failures, asynchronous communication, retries with idempotency, and the various consistency considerations that single-machine programs can ignore. Engineers who have not internalized this discipline write distributed programs that are subtly wrong in ways that emerge only under failure or load, and the cost of these errors is often catastrophic in production systems.

The position within the four axes of Chapter 1 is on the system axis primarily, with substantial engagement on the data axis (because most distributed systems manage data) and on the computation axis (because the algorithmic content of distributed systems is substantial). The intelligence axis is engaged increasingly through the role of distributed systems in serving machine-learning workloads at scale, but the foundational treatment of distributed systems is largely independent of intelligence concerns.

### What you become after studying it

A reader who has internalized distributed systems acquires several capacities that are difficult to acquire by other routes.

The first is *the recognition of distributed-systems problems when they occur*. Many problems in contemporary software involve distributed systems — any system with multiple processes communicating over networks, any system with multiple machines coordinating to provide a service, any system with replicated state. Engineers without distributed-systems training often fail to recognize when their problems are distributed-systems problems, and they apply single-machine intuitions that produce subtly wrong solutions. Engineers with the training recognize the problems immediately and apply the appropriate techniques. This recognition is one of the highest-leverage skills in contemporary software engineering, and it transfers across system domains: the same recognition skill applies to distributed databases, microservice architectures, real-time collaboration systems, and many other settings.

The second capacity is *fluency with the canonical impossibility results*. The FLP impossibility result, the CAP theorem, the various lower bounds on consensus and broadcast — these establish the boundaries of what distributed systems can achieve, and engineers who know them avoid wasting effort on impossible designs. An engineer who tries to build a system that provides strong consistency, perfect availability, and partition tolerance has not understood CAP and will produce a system that fails under partition. An engineer who tries to build a consensus protocol that guarantees both safety and liveness in an asynchronous system with failures has not understood FLP and will produce a protocol that fails to terminate under specific conditions. The canonical impossibility results are not academic curiosities; they are practical constraints that shape what distributed systems can be, and fluency with them is one of the markers of distributed-systems competence.

The third capacity is *the discipline of failure analysis*. The trained practitioner, when designing or evaluating a distributed system, performs systematic failure analysis: what happens if this machine crashes, what happens if this network link fails, what happens if these two events happen simultaneously, what happens if the failures persist for varying durations. The analysis is not exhaustive — no engineer can enumerate all failure scenarios — but it is systematic, and it produces designs that handle the common failures gracefully and that fail in predictable rather than chaotic ways under uncommon failures. Engineers who lack this discipline produce systems whose failure behavior is essentially undefined, and these systems fail in ways that are difficult to predict, diagnose, or remediate.

The fourth capacity is *the appropriate use of consistency models*. The trained practitioner can articulate what consistency model a system requires, what consistency model a particular technology provides, and how to bridge any gap between requirement and provision. They can recognize when an application's correctness depends on properties the underlying system does not provide, and they can either change the application's design to require less or build the missing properties at the application level. Engineers without this competence either over-specify (using strongly consistent systems where weaker consistency would suffice, sacrificing availability and performance unnecessarily) or under-specify (using eventually consistent systems where stronger consistency is required, producing application-level bugs that are difficult to diagnose).

The fifth capacity is *the ability to read distributed systems papers*. The literature of distributed systems is extensive, much of it in the form of conference papers describing specific systems or specific results. The papers have characteristic conventions — system models, fault assumptions, correctness arguments, performance evaluations — that are not transparent to readers without training. After distributed-systems study, the papers become accessible: the practitioner can read them, evaluate their claims, and incorporate their results into their own thinking. This accessibility is what allows the trained engineer to follow contemporary developments and to engage with the research frontier when their work requires it.

The sixth capacity is *humility about distributed systems*. Engineers who have studied distributed systems seriously develop a healthy distrust of distributed systems, even those they have built themselves. They expect failures to occur in ways they did not anticipate, they expect their assumptions to be violated by edge cases they did not consider, and they design accordingly with monitoring, defensive programming, and graceful degradation. This humility is one of the most consistently visible markers of distributed-systems competence, and it is acquired only through the combination of theoretical study and practical experience with distributed systems failing in unexpected ways.

### How the field sees the world

Distributed systems embeds a worldview that is unusually distinctive among CS subjects, partly because the subject is so close to its own theoretical foundations and partly because the contemporary practice of computing is so deeply distributed-systems-shaped.

The first commitment is *to the asynchrony of communication*. The distributed-systems worldview takes for granted that messages between machines take variable amounts of time, may be lost or duplicated, and may be delivered in arbitrary orders. This commitment is not pessimistic — it is realistic. The mechanisms that distributed systems use to achieve coordination operate within these constraints, and they cannot operate as if the constraints did not exist. Engineers without this commitment write distributed code that works in testing (where messages typically arrive quickly and reliably) and fails in production (where the realistic communication patterns occur). The mature practitioner designs from the assumption that communication is asynchronous and unreliable, and treats any apparent reliability as an emergent property of the protocols rather than as a basic guarantee.

The second commitment is *to the centrality of partial failure*. The distinguishing feature of distributed systems, compared to single-machine systems, is that some components can fail while others continue operating. This partial-failure mode is fundamentally different from the all-or-nothing failure mode of single-machine systems: it requires the surviving components to detect the failure (which is itself difficult), to coordinate their response (which is itself a distributed-systems problem), and to continue providing service despite the reduced capacity. The distributed-systems worldview takes partial failure as the dominant failure mode and designs accordingly. Engineers without this commitment design systems that work when all components are healthy and fail catastrophically when any component fails, and these systems are unsuitable for production deployment at scale.

The third commitment is *to the impossibility of perfect failure detection*. In an asynchronous distributed system, no protocol can reliably distinguish a failed machine from a slow machine. A machine that has not responded for a second may be busy, may be experiencing high latency, may have crashed, may have been disconnected from the network. The canonical observation is that "the network is partitioned" and "the other machine has crashed" are indistinguishable from the perspective of a process that is waiting for a response. The mechanisms for handling this — timeouts, heartbeats, the various failure detector abstractions — all involve guesses that may be wrong, and the protocols built on top of failure detectors must be correct even when the failure detector occasionally errs. This commitment shapes how distributed protocols are designed, and the design discipline it produces is one of the most distinctive features of the field.

The fourth commitment is *to the need for explicit coordination*. In single-machine computation, coordination is mostly free — shared memory provides instant communication, atomic operations are cheap, and the operating system manages contention. In distributed computation, coordination is expensive: every coordination operation requires messages, and messages take time and can fail. The distributed-systems worldview treats coordination as a scarce resource to be used deliberately. Designs that minimize coordination scale better than designs that require coordination for every operation, and the discipline of identifying which operations actually require coordination — and designing the rest to proceed without it — is one of the central activities of distributed-systems work. The CALM theorem, which characterizes which distributed computations can proceed without coordination, is a recent formalization of this long-standing engineering intuition.

The fifth commitment is *to the priority of operational considerations*. Distributed systems are not just designs but operating artifacts, and a substantial portion of distributed-systems work involves the operational concerns that single-machine systems can largely ignore: how to deploy software updates without downtime, how to detect anomalies in production traffic, how to debug problems that span multiple machines, how to recover from incidents that affect production users. The mature distributed-systems practitioner takes these operational concerns as integral to the discipline rather than as separate from it, and treats operational considerations as design constraints that shape the architecture from the beginning rather than as concerns to be addressed after deployment.

These five commitments — to asynchrony, to partial failure, to imperfect failure detection, to explicit coordination, and to operational priority — distinguish the distributed-systems worldview from worldviews in adjacent CS subjects. Practitioners who have absorbed them treat distributed computing as a fundamentally different kind of activity than centralized computing; practitioners who have not often try to apply single-machine intuitions to distributed problems and produce systems that fail in predictable ways.

### Common pitfalls in learning it

The pitfalls of learning distributed systems are among the most well-documented in the literature on systems education, and the most damaging are particular to the subject's combination of conceptual depth, engineering complexity, and rapid contemporary evolution.

The most common pitfall is *the eight-fallacies trap*, named for Peter Deutsch's "Eight Fallacies of Distributed Computing": the network is reliable, latency is zero, bandwidth is infinite, the network is secure, topology doesn't change, there is one administrator, transport cost is zero, the network is homogeneous. Engineers without distributed-systems training believe these things, often unconsciously, and design systems that depend on them. The systems work in testing (where the fallacies are nearly true) and fail in production (where they are not). The countermeasure is to internalize the fallacies as anti-patterns — designs that violate any of them are likely wrong — and to systematically check designs against them.

A second pitfall is *the synchronous-RPC mindset*. Remote procedure calls (RPC) are designed to make remote calls look like local calls, and at one level this is a useful abstraction. At another level it is dangerous, because it encourages engineers to think of remote operations as having the properties of local operations: bounded latency, reliable execution, atomic effects. None of these properties hold for actual remote operations, and code written as if they did fails under realistic network conditions. The countermeasure is to recognize the RPC abstraction as a useful tool for some purposes but a dangerous illusion for others, and to write code that handles the realities of remote operation (variable latency, potential failures, partial completion) explicitly.

A third pitfall is *consensus minimization without sufficient understanding*. The advice to "minimize consensus" is widely repeated in distributed-systems literature, and it is correct as a design principle. But minimizing consensus is harder than it sounds: it requires understanding which operations actually require consensus and which can proceed with weaker coordination. Engineers who try to minimize consensus without the underlying understanding often produce systems that have insufficient coordination and behave incorrectly. The countermeasure is to first understand what consensus is for and when it is required, and then to apply the minimization principle within the bounds of that understanding.

A fourth pitfall is *the CAP-theorem misunderstanding*. The CAP theorem states that under network partitions, a distributed system must choose between consistency and availability. This statement is widely repeated but often misunderstood: the theorem is about partitions specifically, not about a general tradeoff between consistency and availability in normal operation. Many engineers internalize a vague version of CAP that holds that distributed systems must always sacrifice either consistency or availability, which is not what the theorem says. The PACELC theorem clarifies the situation: under partitions, choose between availability and consistency; in normal operation, choose between latency and consistency. The countermeasure is to engage with the actual content of CAP (and PACELC) carefully, rather than relying on shorthand summaries.

A fifth pitfall is *ignoring the system model*. Distributed-systems results — both algorithms and impossibility results — are stated relative to specific system models, and the validity of the results depends on the system model assumed. A protocol that is correct in a synchronous model may fail in an asynchronous model. An impossibility result for asynchronous systems may not apply to partially synchronous systems. Engineers who do not pay attention to the system model often misapply results, either trusting protocols that fail in their actual setting or treating possible designs as impossible because they have applied the wrong impossibility result. The countermeasure is to take system models seriously, to be precise about what model one is working in, and to verify that any result one applies is valid in that model.

A sixth pitfall is *the microservices-as-architecture trap*. The microservices architecture has been widely adopted in contemporary software development, and many engineers approach distributed systems primarily through microservices. The trap is that microservices is a specific architectural pattern with specific tradeoffs, and treating it as the answer to distributed-systems concerns produces systems that have all the difficulties of distributed systems without necessarily addressing the underlying problems that distributed systems is designed to solve. Microservices systems can be well-designed (when the service boundaries align with actual independent concerns and the inter-service coordination is minimized) or poorly-designed (when the boundaries are arbitrary and the services require constant coordination); the latter case is unfortunately common. The countermeasure is to evaluate microservices architectures on their merits relative to distributed-systems principles, rather than treating "microservices" as itself a complete answer.

A seventh pitfall is *the local-development illusion*. Distributed systems are difficult to test in local development environments — the failures and timing variations that distinguish distributed from local computation typically do not appear when everything runs on a single development machine. Engineers who develop distributed systems primarily in local environments and deploy to production without serious testing under realistic conditions encounter problems in production that they could have caught earlier. The countermeasure is to test distributed systems under realistic conditions: deliberately injecting failures (chaos engineering), testing under realistic latency and bandwidth conditions, running large-scale tests before deployment.

An eighth pitfall is *the chasing-of-distributed-systems-fashion trap*. The distributed-systems community has been continuously productive of new architectural patterns, new technologies, and new buzzwords: cloud-native, microservices, service mesh, serverless, distributed monolith, event-driven architecture, CQRS, event sourcing, the various blockchain-related architectures. Each pattern has contexts where it is appropriate and contexts where it is not, but the broader culture often presents each as the answer rather than as one option among many. Engineers who chase fashion produce systems that are over-engineered for their actual requirements and that incorporate technologies whose tradeoffs the engineers have not internalized. The countermeasure is to evaluate each pattern on its specific merits for specific use cases, recognizing that the costs of distribution are real and should not be incurred without specific justification.

### The literature of the subject

Distributed systems has a more recently consolidated literature than older subjects. The classical foundations are stable, but the contemporary applied literature has developed substantially over the past fifteen years, and the consolidation is still in progress. The selections below cover both the classical foundations and the contemporary applied work.

#### Canonical entry points

The most useful contemporary entry to distributed systems is Martin Kleppmann's *Designing Data-Intensive Applications*, already mentioned in Section 2.8 as a database text. The book is also one of the best contemporary distributed-systems books, covering the major problems of distributed systems (replication, partitioning, consistency, transactions in distributed systems) at a level appropriate for working engineers and with substantial engagement with the underlying research literature. The book has become the standard contemporary reference for engineers entering distributed systems, and it is widely regarded as one of the best technical books published in the past decade.

The classical entry to distributed systems, with substantially more theoretical orientation, is Tanenbaum and van Steen's *Distributed Systems*. The book covers the foundational material — system models, communication, processes, naming, synchronization, consistency and replication, fault tolerance, security — at a level appropriate for an academic course. The third edition is freely available online from the authors. Tanenbaum is more comprehensive than Kleppmann in its theoretical coverage but less engaging for working engineers, and the two books complement each other.

For the algorithmic and theoretical side of distributed systems, the canonical text is Lynch's *Distributed Algorithms*. The book is mathematically rigorous, covers the major algorithms (consensus, mutual exclusion, leader election, broadcast, snapshots, the various replication algorithms) at depth, and presents the impossibility results with their proofs. It is more demanding than the systems-oriented texts and is appropriate for learners with stronger mathematical preparation, but it is the right reference for anyone who wants the algorithmic foundation rather than only the engineering foundation.

The MIT 6.5840 (formerly 6.824) Distributed Systems course is freely available online and is among the most highly regarded distributed-systems courses anywhere. The course covers the foundational material through reading assignments (using key papers in the field) and through a substantial implementation project (typically implementing Raft and a sharded key-value store). Working through the course materials systematically is one of the most effective ways to acquire distributed-systems competence, and the course has produced a generation of distributed-systems engineers.

The reading order recommendation: Kleppmann as the primary engineering-oriented entry, with Tanenbaum consulted for theoretical material that Kleppmann handles less thoroughly. Lynch for learners who want the algorithmic foundation. The MIT 6.5840 course as the project-based companion to whichever text is the primary. The combination of one of the textbooks and the MIT course, with the implementation labs done seriously, is the standard contemporary preparation.

#### The progression

After the canonical introduction, the progression in distributed systems branches according to specialization.

*Consensus and replication at depth.* The foundational papers on consensus are essential reading: Lamport's "The Part-Time Parliament" (the original Paxos paper, famously difficult to read), Lamport's "Paxos Made Simple" (the more accessible later treatment), Ongaro and Ousterhout's "In Search of an Understandable Consensus Algorithm" (the Raft paper). Beyond Paxos and Raft, the literature on multi-Paxos, Generalized Paxos, EPaxos, and the various Byzantine consensus protocols (PBFT, Tendermint, HotStuff) extends the picture into contemporary practice. Reading these papers systematically — typically a paper a week, with implementation exercises — is the standard advanced study path for consensus.

*Distributed databases.* The papers on Spanner ("Spanner: Google's Globally-Distributed Database"), on Dynamo ("Dynamo: Amazon's Highly Available Key-Value Store"), on the various Bigtable-derived systems (Bigtable, HBase, Cassandra), and on the various contemporary distributed databases (CockroachDB, FoundationDB, TiDB, YugabyteDB) constitute the contemporary literature on distributed databases. Reading these papers gives a sense of how the foundational distributed-systems concepts have been applied to specific production systems, and the differences among the systems illustrate the design space well.

*Stream processing and event-driven architectures.* The papers on Kafka, on Flink, on the various stream-processing systems, and on the conceptual framework of event-driven architectures (the work of Kreps, of Akidau, and others) form a substantial subliterature. Streaming has become one of the major contemporary subareas of distributed systems, and engagement with the literature is appropriate for engineers working in this area.

*Coordination services.* The papers on ZooKeeper, on Chubby, on etcd, and on the various coordination services that underlie modern distributed systems are valuable for understanding how the abstract consensus protocols are realized in production-grade infrastructure components. These services are often the foundation on which other distributed systems are built, and understanding them is essential for engineers working with cluster management, configuration distribution, or service discovery.

*Distributed file systems and storage.* The classical papers on the Google File System, Hadoop, and the various distributed file systems, together with the more recent papers on object storage and on the distributed storage layers of modern cloud providers, form the literature on this area. Distributed storage is one of the most well-understood areas of distributed systems, and the literature is correspondingly mature.

*Blockchain and decentralized systems.* The blockchain literature is substantial but variable in quality. The original Bitcoin paper, the Ethereum yellow paper, the various papers on the consensus mechanisms underlying contemporary blockchains, and the academic literature on Byzantine fault tolerance and decentralized systems form the high-quality core. Much of the broader blockchain discussion is technically shallow or commercially motivated, and engineers approaching this area should be selective about sources.

*Operational distributed systems.* The literature on operating distributed systems in practice — site reliability engineering, observability, chaos engineering, the various deployment and orchestration patterns — is a substantial body of contemporary work. Beyer, Jones, Petoff, and Murphy's *Site Reliability Engineering* and its companion *The Site Reliability Workbook* are the standard texts on the operational side of distributed systems, and they have become widely influential beyond their original Google context.

#### Reference works

Kleppmann's *Designing Data-Intensive Applications*, mentioned above as a primary text, is also a reference work that engineers consult throughout their careers. The book's organization makes it easy to look up specific topics, and the treatment is thorough enough to support reference use.

Lamport's collected papers (available freely from his personal website) are an essential reference for distributed-systems work. Lamport's contributions span four decades and include many of the field's foundational results (logical clocks, Byzantine generals, Paxos, the various papers on shared-memory algorithms). Reading Lamport directly, after introductory study, is illuminating: the papers are unusually clear, the arguments are rigorous, and the historical context is visible.

The proceedings of the major distributed-systems conferences — SOSP, OSDI, NSDI, EuroSys, FAST — are reference works in their own right. Working distributed-systems engineers consult these proceedings regularly when researching specific topics, and the papers are freely available.

The various distributed-systems textbooks — Coulouris, Dollimore, Kindberg, and Blair's *Distributed Systems: Concepts and Design*, Cachin, Guerraoui, and Rodrigues's *Introduction to Reliable and Secure Distributed Programming* — are useful as references for specific topics. Cachin et al. in particular is the most rigorous treatment of the algorithmic foundations and is essential for serious algorithmic work.

#### Heterodox correctives

Lamport's "The Part-Time Parliament" is heterodox in its presentation: the paper is famously written as a fictional history of a Greek island's voting protocol, and reading it is an unusual experience that nonetheless contains the original presentation of Paxos. Lamport's commentary on why he wrote the paper this way (available alongside the paper) is itself worth reading.

Brewer's papers on the CAP theorem and his subsequent reflections (particularly "CAP Twelve Years Later: How the 'Rules' Have Changed") are heterodox in their willingness to revise the original framing as understanding has developed. Reading these papers gives a more nuanced picture of CAP than the standard summaries provide.

The papers on the CALM theorem (by Hellerstein and others) are heterodox in their argument that coordination-free distributed computation is more achievable than the standard distributed-systems framing suggests. The CALM theorem identifies which distributed computations are inherently coordination-free, and the framework it provides is a significant alternative to the consensus-centric mainstream.

Various critical writings on contemporary distributed-systems practice — particularly the "microservices skepticism" literature, the writings of practitioners who have observed how distributed systems fail in practice, the postmortems and incident reports from major outages — are heterodox correctives to the optimistic framings common in commercial distributed-systems literature. Reading this material gives a more realistic picture of what distributed systems are like in production.

Pat Helland's various papers and writings constitute a heterodox commentary on distributed systems from a practitioner's perspective. Helland's papers on data on the outside versus data on the inside, on idempotence, on the various forms of identity in distributed systems, are unusually clear and deeply considered, and they represent a perspective on distributed systems that academic literature does not capture well.

#### What to skip and why

Most introductory distributed-systems books not mentioned above can be skipped if the canonical texts are available. The genre is younger than for older subjects, and the variation is greater, but the canonical options have established themselves clearly.

Books that focus exclusively on specific technologies (the various "Kubernetes in Practice" or "Kafka Internals" books) are appropriate as technology-specific references but should not be confused with distributed-systems study. The technology details are useful for engineers working with those technologies, but they do not substitute for the conceptual foundation.

Most blockchain books published during the cryptocurrency boom of the late 2010s and early 2020s should be approached with caution. Many are technically shallow, commercially motivated, or focused on speculation rather than on the underlying technical content. The exceptions are the formal academic literature and the technical specifications, which are valuable but require selectivity to find.

Books that promise to teach distributed systems through a single vendor's products are typically vendor-specific marketing material disguised as education. They should be skipped except when the specific vendor's products are the engineer's professional concern.

Most online distributed-systems tutorials are uneven in quality, and the field's complexity makes shallow tutorials particularly unhelpful. The exceptions are the high-quality blog posts from working distributed-systems engineers (Aphyr's Jepsen analyses, the various posts from Google, AWS, and Microsoft engineering teams, the writings of independent practitioners like Kingsbury) which are valuable supplements but not substitutes for systematic study.

The "microservices marketing literature" — books and articles that present microservices as the answer to distributed-systems concerns without engaging seriously with the underlying tradeoffs — should be skipped. Microservices is a useful architectural pattern in some contexts, but the literature that presents it as universally applicable is usually shallow.

#### Contemporary sources

The major distributed-systems conferences are SOSP, OSDI, NSDI, EuroSys, and FAST. Their proceedings represent the research frontier and are freely available. Following these proceedings, even superficially, gives a sense of the contemporary research direction.

The various engineering blogs from major distributed-systems-heavy companies are valuable contemporary sources. Google's research publications, AWS's various engineering writings, Microsoft Research's distributed-systems output, and the various engineering blogs from cloud-native companies (Cloudflare, Netflix, Uber, Spotify, and many others) provide insight into how contemporary distributed systems are actually designed and operated.

Kyle Kingsbury's *Jepsen* analyses are unusually valuable: each analysis examines a specific distributed system for correctness violations under failure, and the analyses have repeatedly identified bugs and design flaws in production systems. Reading Jepsen analyses is a particularly effective way to understand what can go wrong in distributed systems, and the analyses have substantially shaped how the broader community approaches distributed-systems correctness.

Murat Demirbas's blog (muratbuffalo.blogspot.com) is one of the highest-quality contemporary sources on distributed-systems research, with regular paper reviews and commentary. Marc Brooker's writing covers distributed-systems topics with depth and clarity. The various academic blogs from active distributed-systems researchers provide insight into the contemporary research perspective.

The CMU Database Group's series on distributed systems (alongside the database series mentioned in Section 2.8) is a high-quality source on contemporary distributed database systems and the underlying distributed-systems concepts.

For operational distributed systems, the *Site Reliability Engineering* book and its successors, together with the various conference talks from the SREcon and similar venues, provide the contemporary literature on operating distributed systems at scale.

#### Reference table

| Resource | Role | Tag |
|---|---|---|
