| Most older PL theory texts | Superseded by contemporary canon | Skip |
| Polemical language-paradigm books | More rhetoric than substance | Skip |

### A note on contemporary relevance

Programming language theory is among the more active subfields of computer science in the late 2020s, with several developments reshaping the field.

The most consequential contemporary development is the continued maturation of dependent types and the emergence of dependently-typed programming as a practical option. Languages like Lean 4, Idris 2, and Agda have become substantially more usable, and the proof assistants (Coq, Lean, Isabelle) have demonstrated that dependently-typed development can be done at scale. The implications for programming language theory are substantial: the tools that the field has developed are becoming increasingly applicable to industrial software development, and the boundary between programming language theory and formal methods is becoming more permeable.

The second major development is the increasing influence of programming language theory on industrial language design. Recent languages — Rust, Swift, Scala 3, Kotlin, the various typed dialects of dynamic languages — have substantially more programming language theoretic content than industrial languages a generation ago. Type systems have become more sophisticated; effect systems are appearing in production languages; the formal foundations of language semantics are increasingly being taken seriously by language designers. The field's influence on practice is greater than it has been in decades.

The third development is the rise of WebAssembly as a target for programming language work. WebAssembly has emerged as a portable, secure compilation target for a wide range of programming languages, and the research on WebAssembly's design (including formal semantics for it) has been a substantial recent contribution of programming language theory. The implications for how languages are implemented and deployed are significant.

The fourth development is the engagement of programming language theory with concurrent and distributed computing. The rise of multicore processors and distributed systems has motivated substantial work on language support for concurrency and distribution: session types, the various typed pi-calculi, the distributed type systems, the work on consistency types. These developments connect programming language theory to distributed systems (Section 2.9) in productive ways.

The fifth development, and the most uncertain, is the role of large language models in programming language theory. The current generation of LLMs can read and generate programming language theoretic notation, can produce passable proofs in proof assistants, and can sometimes propose nonstandard approaches to research problems. The implications for how programming language theory is conducted are not yet clear, but the practice of working programming language theorists is evolving to incorporate AI tools, and the future of the field's working methodology is genuinely open.

Despite these developments, the foundational character of programming language theory is robust. The major calculi remain. The semantic frameworks remain. The Curry-Howard correspondence remains. The intellectual core of the field — the discipline of giving precise meaning to programs — has been refined over decades and continues to be the substance of the subject. A learner who masters the foundational material is equipped to engage with whatever specific developments emerge, and the canonical texts cover this foundation well. The contemporary developments add to the picture but do not replace it.

## 3.2 — Type Theory and the Curry-Howard Correspondence

### What it is, properly

Type theory is the mathematical study of types and the systems that organize them. The subject sits at a peculiar position in the landscape of intellectual disciplines: it is simultaneously a branch of mathematical logic, a foundation for programming language design, an alternative foundation for mathematics itself, and a practical technology for software verification. These four roles are not separate applications of a single subject; they are different perspectives on the same underlying mathematical content, and the recognition that they are the same is one of the most consequential intellectual achievements of twentieth-century computer science.

The conventional account of type theory treats it as a feature of programming languages — the mechanism by which programming languages classify values and prevent certain runtime errors. This account captures the most visible application of type theory but misses what makes the subject foundational. The conceptual core of type theory is not the prevention of runtime errors, useful as that is, but the recognition that *types are propositions and programs are proofs*. This is the Curry-Howard correspondence, named for Haskell Curry and William Howard, who developed the recognition independently in the mid-twentieth century: under suitable interpretations, the rules that govern well-typed programs are exactly the rules that govern valid logical proofs, and the two are the same mathematical content viewed under different names.

The correspondence is more than a curiosity. It establishes that programming and proving are not analogous activities but the same activity, that the type systems of programming languages can be evaluated as logical systems, and that the proof assistants that construct mathematical proofs can be evaluated as programming languages. The contemporary discipline of type theory works out the implications of this recognition systematically: developing type systems that are simultaneously useful programming language type systems and useful logical systems, building proof assistants that exploit this duality, and using type theory as a foundation for both software verification and mathematical formalization.

The content of type theory, examined seriously, is structured around several layers. At the bottom is the *simply typed lambda calculus*, the simplest type system that captures the essential idea — a calculus in which every expression has a type, types are formed by basic types and function types, and the rules for type checking and type formation correspond exactly to the rules of intuitionistic propositional logic. At the next layer is *polymorphic type theory*, exemplified by System F, in which types can be parameterized by other types and which corresponds to second-order propositional logic. Above this are the *dependently typed* systems, exemplified by Martin-Löf type theory and the calculus of constructions, in which types can depend on values and which correspond to first-order and higher-order predicate logic. At the contemporary frontier are *homotopy type theory* and the various recent developments that extend type theory in increasingly sophisticated directions.

The intellectual achievement of type theory is the construction of formal systems in which programming and proving are the same activity, in which the type system enforces correctness through a discipline that is simultaneously logical and computational, and in which the resulting artifacts are both executable programs and verified proofs. This achievement has been refined over half a century and is now being deployed in industrial software development with increasing frequency. The implications for how programs are written and verified are substantial, and they are continuing to develop.

### The deep problems the field tries to answer

Three problems organize type theory as a discipline.

**The problem of correctness through structure.** A type system is a discipline that the programmer accepts in exchange for guarantees about the resulting programs. The simplest type systems prevent the simplest classes of errors (operations applied to inappropriate values, mismatched function arguments, references to undefined names). More sophisticated type systems prevent more sophisticated errors: inappropriate concurrent access, resource leaks, security violations, deviations from arbitrarily specified behavioral properties. The fundamental question of type theory is how far this trajectory extends — what kinds of properties can be expressed in type systems and what the cost of expressing them is. The answer, which has emerged over decades of work, is that essentially any mathematically expressible property can be expressed in suitable type systems, that the cost of doing so depends on the property, and that the field's continuing work is largely the activity of finding type systems that express important properties at acceptable cost.

**The problem of foundational adequacy.** Mathematics has traditionally been founded on set theory (Zermelo-Fraenkel set theory with the axiom of choice, in the standard contemporary version), with logical apparatus drawn from first-order logic. Type theory offers an alternative foundation, in which types replace sets and dependent type theory replaces first-order logic. The alternative is not merely cosmetic — type theoretic foundations have different properties than set theoretic foundations, particularly in their handling of constructive content (type theory is fundamentally constructive, while set theory accommodates classical reasoning more naturally) and in their relationship to computation (type theory is computational by construction, while set theory has a more distant relationship to computation). Whether type theory should replace set theory as the foundation of mathematics is a live question, with substantial communities advocating each position. The contemporary work in homotopy type theory, in particular, has pushed the type-theoretic foundational program forward substantially, and the question of whether type theory is the right foundation is increasingly answered with cautious optimism.

**The problem of mechanized proof.** A proof, traditionally, is a piece of mathematical argument that a human reads and judges to be correct. A mechanized proof is a piece of formal data that a computer checks for correctness, ruling out the possibility of human error in the verification. The technology for mechanized proof has matured substantially over the past three decades, and proof assistants based on type theory (Coq, Lean, Agda, Isabelle, the various less prominent alternatives) are now capable of verifying substantial mathematical and computational artifacts. The four-color theorem has been mechanically verified. The Feit-Thompson theorem has been mechanically verified. The CompCert C compiler is mechanically verified. The seL4 microkernel is mechanically verified. These are not academic exercises — they are real artifacts of substantial complexity, and their mechanical verification represents a level of correctness assurance that no other technique can provide. The continuing work of type theory is largely the activity of making mechanized proof more tractable, more applicable to a wider range of problems, and more accessible to practitioners who are not specialists in type theory.

### Where it sits in the map

Type theory occupies a central position on the theoretical axis. It depends on the theory of computation (Section 2.3) for its foundational apparatus, on programming language theory (Section 3.1) for the context in which it is most often applied, and on logic (Section 3.5) for the side of the Curry-Howard correspondence that is not directly about computation. It connects to category theory (Section 3.3) through the categorical semantics of types (which is one of the major contemporary frameworks for understanding type theory), to formal methods (Section 3.4) through the proof assistants in which type theoretic foundations are deployed, and to programming languages generally through the type systems that are increasingly central to language design.

The relationship to programming language theory (the subject of Section 3.1) is closer than for any other pair of subjects in the theoretical axis. Type theory is, at one level, a substantial subarea of programming language theory — the part that concerns type systems specifically. At another level, it is a separate discipline with its own concerns, its own community, and its own literature, distinct from the rest of programming language theory. The dual character is real and reflects the field's history: type theory developed in mathematical logic before computer science existed as a discipline, and it has retained its mathematical character even as it has been incorporated into computer science. Practitioners who come to type theory from programming language theory tend to emphasize its programming-language applications; practitioners who come to it from mathematical logic tend to emphasize its foundational role; both perspectives are legitimate, and the field benefits from their coexistence.

The relationship to mathematics is unusual. Most CS subjects use mathematics as a tool while remaining distinct from mathematics. Type theory is, in a real sense, a branch of mathematics — many of its practitioners hold appointments in mathematics departments, many of its results are theorems in mathematical logic, and the boundary between type theory and constructive mathematics is largely conventional. This positioning has consequences for how the subject is approached: serious type theory requires the mathematical maturity that the rest of computer science does not always require, and learners coming from a more engineering-oriented background often find the subject more demanding than other CS specializations.

The position within the four axes of Chapter 1 is squarely on the computation axis, with substantial engagement on what might be called the mathematical-logic axis (which is not one of the four primary axes but is closely related to the computation axis). Type theory is the subject in which computation and logic merge most fully, and the merger is what gives the subject its distinctive intellectual content.

### What you become after studying it

A reader who has internalized type theory acquires several capacities that are unusual among programmers and that have substantial professional value.

The first is *fluency with the program-proof correspondence*. The trained type theorist sees programs and proofs as the same kind of object, viewed under different names. When writing a program, they see what proposition the program would prove if interpreted as a proof; when constructing a proof, they see what program the proof would be if interpreted as a program. This dual vision is one of the most consequential cognitive shifts that any computer science subject produces, and it transfers across many other activities. Practitioners who hold this view tend to write programs whose correctness is more apparent (because the programs reflect the structure of arguments rather than the structure of computations), and to construct arguments whose computational content is more apparent (because the arguments are constructive in form).

The second capacity is *the ability to use proof assistants*. Proof assistants based on type theory (Coq, Lean, Agda, Isabelle/HOL) are substantial pieces of software that take significant time to learn to use effectively. The trained type theorist can use these tools — can formalize mathematical content in them, can verify properties of programs in them, can engage with the substantial libraries of mathematics and computer science that have been formalized in them. This capacity is increasingly valuable as proof assistants become more central to high-assurance software development and to the foundational mathematics community.

The third capacity is *fluency with dependent types*. Dependent types — types that depend on values — are the central technical content of contemporary type theory and represent a substantial generalization beyond the type systems of mainstream programming languages. The trained type theorist thinks naturally in terms of dependent types: they see how a type might be parameterized by a value, how a function's return type might depend on its arguments, how invariants might be encoded in types so that the type checker enforces them. This fluency is acquired through extended practice and is one of the markers of serious type theoretic competence.

The fourth capacity is *the discipline of constructive thinking*. Type theory is fundamentally constructive — proofs in type theory are constructions of objects that demonstrate the propositions, rather than arguments that propositions hold abstractly. This discipline transfers to programming generally: the constructive type theorist writes code that constructs what is needed rather than testing whether something exists, that returns evidence of computation rather than just results, that handles cases by constructing handlers rather than by raising errors. This style of programming, when applied consistently, produces code with characteristic properties: it tends to be more verbose initially but more reliable in the long run, and it tends to be more amenable to formal verification when verification becomes important.

The fifth capacity is *the ability to read type theoretic papers and formalizations*. The literature of type theory is substantial and uses notation that is initially impenetrable to readers without training. After serious study, the literature becomes accessible, and the practitioner can engage with the contemporary research frontier when their work requires it. The various formalizations available in proof assistants — the mathlib library in Lean, the Coq Standard Library, the Agda standard library — become readable, and the practitioner can use these as resources for their own work. This accessibility is what allows the trained practitioner to participate in the contemporary developments rather than being dependent on others' summaries.

### How the field sees the world

Type theory embeds a worldview that is unusually distinct, both because the subject is so far from engineering practice and because its commitments are unusually consequential.

The first commitment is *to constructivity*. Type theory holds that mathematical objects exist when they can be constructed, and that proofs are constructions rather than arguments. This commitment runs against the classical mathematical disposition, which accepts proofs by contradiction (showing that the negation of a proposition leads to absurdity, without constructing what the proposition asserts) and other non-constructive techniques. The constructivity commitment has consequences: many classical theorems are not directly provable in type theory, classical reasoning must be added explicitly when needed, and the resulting mathematics has a different texture than classical mathematics. The trained type theorist accepts the constructivity discipline, sees it as a feature rather than a limitation, and recognizes the mathematics that emerges as having computational content that classical mathematics often lacks.

The second commitment is *to the centrality of types as propositions*. The Curry-Howard correspondence is not merely a technical observation but a foundational commitment: types and propositions are the same kind of thing, and the apparent differences between them are matters of perspective. This commitment shapes how type theorists approach programming: they see type systems as logical systems, they evaluate type system features by their logical properties, and they hold that good type systems should correspond to coherent logical systems. The commitment also shapes how type theorists approach mathematics: they see propositions as types, they expect mathematical content to have computational interpretation, and they hold that mathematics conducted in type theory is more intellectually disciplined than mathematics conducted in informal natural language.

The third commitment is *to the unity of programming and proving*. Beyond the technical Curry-Howard correspondence is a broader commitment to the view that programming and proving are aspects of a single activity. The mature type theorist programs and proves continuously, switching between the two activities as the situation demands, treating them as the same kind of work conducted in different registers. This unity has practical consequences: programs are written with their correctness arguments in mind, and proofs are constructed with their computational content in mind. The result is a style of work in which correctness is built into artifacts rather than verified after the fact, and in which mathematical content is rendered computational rather than treated as separate from computation.

The fourth commitment is *to mechanization*. Type theorists hold that mathematical and computational arguments should be mechanizable — should be representable in formal systems that computers can check — and that the discipline of mechanization improves the quality of arguments. This commitment is partly empirical (mechanized arguments have been found to be more reliable than non-mechanized arguments) and partly philosophical (the mechanization discipline imposes precision that informal practice often lacks). The commitment leads type theorists to invest substantial effort in formalizing material in proof assistants, to evaluate the formalizability of arguments as a quality measure, and to be skeptical of arguments that resist mechanization.

These four commitments — to constructivity, to the centrality of types as propositions, to the unity of programming and proving, to mechanization — distinguish the type theoretic worldview from worldviews in adjacent CS subjects and from worldviews in classical mathematics. Practitioners who have absorbed them treat programming and proving as deeply integrated activities, and the integration shapes how they engage with both software development and mathematical content.

### Common pitfalls in learning it

The pitfalls of learning type theory are particular to its position between programming and mathematics, and the most damaging are characteristic of the subject.

The most common pitfall is *the type-system-feature trap*. Many programmers come to type theory through programming language type systems, and their initial engagement is in terms of specific features: generics, type inference, traits or type classes, the various forms of polymorphism. This is a legitimate entry point, but it can produce a fragmented understanding in which type theory appears as a collection of disjointed features rather than as a coherent mathematical subject. The countermeasure is to engage with the foundational material — the simply typed lambda calculus, the systematic development of more sophisticated type systems on this foundation — rather than approaching type theory primarily through specific language features.

A second pitfall is *the proof-assistant trap*. Some learners approach type theory primarily through proof assistants (Coq, Lean, Agda), expecting that the tools will teach them the underlying theory. The tools are pedagogically valuable but are not substitutes for the conceptual material: they are sophisticated software systems with substantial learning curves of their own, and learning the tools without learning the theory produces tool fluency without conceptual understanding. The countermeasure is to study the theory alongside the tool — to read TAPL or Pierce's lecture notes while working through Coq's tutorial, to study Martin-Löf type theory while working through Agda's introductory material.

A third pitfall is *the dependent-types-immediately trap*. Dependent types are the most distinctive feature of contemporary type theory and are correspondingly tempting as a starting point. They are also among the most difficult features to engage with: dependent types require comfort with both the type-theoretic apparatus and the logical content that the apparatus expresses, and engaging with them before having either is unproductive. The countermeasure is to follow the standard pedagogical sequence: simply typed lambda calculus first, polymorphic type theory second (System F), dependent types only after these foundations are solid. The temptation to skip ahead is real, but the result is typically confusion rather than acceleration.

A fourth pitfall is *the foundational-debate distraction*. Type theorists frequently engage in foundational debates: whether type theory should replace set theory as the foundation of mathematics, whether classical reasoning should be admitted, whether univalent foundations should be adopted, whether specific design decisions in proof assistants are correct. These debates are intellectually serious and matter for the field's development, but they can absorb learner attention disproportionately and produce the impression that type theory is fundamentally about the debates rather than about the underlying mathematics. The countermeasure is to treat the foundational debates as one aspect of the field rather than as its center, and to engage with the working mathematics of type theory primarily.

A fifth pitfall is *the proof-engineering-without-theory trap*. Proof assistants enable a form of work — proof engineering — that is partially separate from theoretical type theory. A proof engineer can become productive with a proof assistant without engaging deeply with the underlying type theory, treating the assistant as a tool whose internal workings need not be understood in depth. This is a legitimate professional path, but it has a ceiling: proof engineers who do not understand the underlying theory hit limitations they cannot diagnose, struggle with proof assistant features whose theoretical motivation they do not grasp, and produce formalizations that are correct but unidiomatic. The countermeasure is to study the theory alongside the engineering, recognizing that the two complement each other.

A sixth pitfall is *the practical-applicability discouragement*. Type theory has fewer immediate applications to mainstream software development than other theoretical-axis subjects, and learners sometimes become discouraged when they cannot see direct practical use for what they are learning. The discouragement is partly justified — type theory's industrial applications, while growing, are still concentrated in specific domains (high-assurance software, certain compilers, certain language runtimes) — but it is partly misplaced. The intellectual content of type theory is valuable in itself, the cognitive shifts it produces transfer broadly, and its industrial applications are growing rapidly. The countermeasure is to recognize that some intellectual investments pay off over careers rather than over months, and that type theory is one of these.

A seventh pitfall, increasingly relevant, is *the LLM-shortcut problem*. Modern AI tools can produce passable type theoretic content — can complete proof obligations, can write code in dependently typed languages, can explain type theoretic concepts. Learners who use these tools to bypass the cognitive struggle of constructing proofs and of working through type theoretic exercises forfeit much of the learning. Type theory is one of the subjects in which the cognitive struggle is most clearly the substance of the learning: a proof that has been worked out by struggle is understood at a level that a proof produced by an LLM is not. The discipline required is the same discipline required for the rest of mathematical study: do early work without LLM assistance, introduce AI tools only after the underlying skill has been built, and use AI tools as amplifiers of judgment rather than substitutes for it.

### The literature of the subject

Type theory has a substantial but specialized literature, with several works occupying clear canonical positions.

#### Canonical entry points

The canonical entry to type theory in its programming-language-theoretic aspect is Pierce's *Types and Programming Languages* (TAPL), already discussed in Section 3.1 as the canonical entry to programming language theory. TAPL is also the right entry for type theory specifically: the book develops the major typed lambda calculi systematically and culminates in a sketch of dependent types that motivates further study. For most learners approaching type theory from a programming background, TAPL is the appropriate starting point.

For learners approaching type theory from a mathematical-logic background, or those who want a more foundational treatment, the canonical entry is Martin-Löf's *Intuitionistic Type Theory* (the lecture notes from the 1980 Padua lectures, freely available in various forms). Martin-Löf's notes are short, dense, and historically central — they introduced the type theory that has subsequently become foundational to much of the contemporary field. Reading Martin-Löf directly is illuminating, though the writing is austere and assumes substantial mathematical maturity. The notes are the right entry for foundationally oriented learners.

The contemporary standard text on dependent type theory is the *Homotopy Type Theory* book (the HoTT book), produced collaboratively by the Univalent Foundations Program and freely available online. The book is comprehensive, covers the contemporary state of the field, and is the right reference for the modern type theoretic landscape. It is mathematically demanding and is appropriate for learners who have completed introductory study, not as a first encounter.

For an unusually accessible entry to dependent type theory through programming, Christiansen and Bove's *Programming Language Foundations in Agda* (free online, often called the "PLFA book") is appropriate. The book is structured as a tutorial in the Agda proof assistant that simultaneously develops the underlying type theory. For learners who want to engage with type theory through hands-on work in a proof assistant, PLFA is the standard contemporary choice.

The reading order recommendation: TAPL as the primary text for most learners, supplemented by Martin-Löf's lectures for the foundational perspective. The HoTT book as the contemporary frontier reference after the foundations are in place. PLFA or Stump's *Verified Functional Programming in Agda* as the hands-on companion for learners who want to engage with proof assistants. For learners specifically pursuing the Lean proof assistant, the *Theorem Proving in Lean 4* tutorial and the *Mathematics in Lean* book (both freely available) are the appropriate equivalents.

#### The progression

After the canonical introduction, the progression in type theory branches according to specialization.

*Foundational type theory.* Beyond Martin-Löf's lectures, the standard advanced references are Nordström, Petersson, and Smith's *Programming in Martin-Löf's Type Theory: An Introduction* (free online), and the various papers of Martin-Löf and his successors. These materials develop the foundational program in detail and are appropriate for learners pursuing type theory as foundation rather than as application.

*Homotopy type theory.* Beyond the HoTT book, the contemporary literature on homotopy type theory is substantial and accessible. Awodey's various papers on homotopy type theory are clearly written. The various works of Voevodsky (whose Univalent Foundations program initiated much of the contemporary work) are essential reading for the historical and conceptual context. The continuing development of cubical type theory and the various extensions of homotopy type theory is an active area, and engagement with the recent literature is appropriate for serious specialists.

*Coq and CIC.* The Calculus of Inductive Constructions (CIC), the type theory underlying Coq, has its own substantial literature. Bertot and Castéran's *Interactive Theorem Proving and Program Development: Coq'Art* is the standard reference for working with CIC and Coq. Chlipala's *Certified Programming with Dependent Types* is a more recent text that develops both Coq and substantial verified software. For more recent Coq material, the *Software Foundations* series (originally by Pierce and collaborators, now by a larger consortium) is freely available and is among the best programming-oriented introductions to mechanized verification.

*Lean and mathlib.* The Lean proof assistant, particularly in its current Lean 4 version, has emerged as a major contemporary platform for mathematical formalization. Avigad and Massot's *Mathematics in Lean* is the standard introduction for mathematicians approaching Lean. The mathlib library — the Lean community's effort to formalize substantial mathematics — is itself an educational resource, and engaging with its codebase teaches Lean and contemporary type theoretic mathematics simultaneously. The Lean community is unusually welcoming to newcomers, and the documentation, tutorials, and Zulip chat are valuable resources.

*Agda and type-driven development.* Beyond PLFA and Stump's book, Brady's *Type-Driven Development with Idris* is the standard text on type-driven development as a programming methodology. The methodology — building programs by progressively refining types until the implementation is essentially determined — is one of the practical applications of dependent types and is appropriate for learners who want to apply type theoretic ideas to programming work.

*Linear and affine types.* Linear logic and the corresponding linear and affine type systems have substantial recent applications, particularly in Rust's borrow checker and in the various session-typed approaches to concurrent programming. Girard's original papers on linear logic are dense but illuminating. Wadler's various papers on linear types in functional programming are accessible introductions. Pottier and Protzenko's work on Mezzo and on F* extends linear types in various directions.

*Effect types.* Effect systems — type systems that track computational effects (state changes, exceptions, IO, nondeterminism) at the type level — have emerged as a substantial recent direction in type theory. The Koka language and its associated papers are the most accessible introduction to algebraic effects and effect handlers. Plotkin and Pretnar's papers on algebraic effects are the foundational technical literature.

#### Reference works

TAPL and the HoTT book, mentioned above, are the standard reference works. The various proof assistant manuals and reference documentation (the Coq reference manual, the Lean documentation, the Agda manual) are reference works in their own right and are consulted regularly by working practitioners.

The proceedings of POPL, ICFP, LICS, FSCD, and the various more specialized conferences (TYPES, MFPS) are reference works for the contemporary state of the field.

The various community libraries — the mathlib in Lean, the Coq libraries, the Agda standard library, the Isabelle Archive of Formal Proofs — are simultaneously reference works and active research artifacts. Reading these libraries is an unusual but valuable activity: it shows what serious contemporary type theoretic mathematics looks like in practice.

#### Heterodox correctives

Voevodsky's various papers and lectures, particularly those on the motivations for univalent foundations, are heterodox in their willingness to question established foundational practices. Voevodsky's Fields Medal-winning work in algebraic geometry combined with his late-career commitment to univalent foundations gives unusual weight to his foundational arguments, and engaging with his thinking is illuminating for understanding why the contemporary type theoretic community has invested so heavily in univalent foundations.

The various papers on cubical type theory (by Cohen, Coquand, Huber, Mörtberg and others) are heterodox in their technical approach: they provide computational interpretations of univalent foundations that the original homotopy type theory did not have, and they represent the contemporary cutting edge of foundational type theory. Engaging with this literature is appropriate for advanced learners.

Constructive mathematics from non-type-theoretic perspectives — Bishop's *Foundations of Constructive Analysis*, Bridges and Vita's *Techniques of Constructive Analysis*, the various papers on Brouwer's intuitionism — is heterodox in providing constructive mathematical perspective without the type-theoretic apparatus. Reading this material gives a different perspective on what constructive mathematics can be.

Bob Harper's blog posts on type theory and on the Curry-Howard correspondence, while opinionated, are heterodox in their willingness to make substantive arguments about how type theory should be done and taught. Engaging with Harper's arguments sharpens one's own thinking even when one disagrees.

The various critiques of type theory from the classical mathematical perspective (Mac Lane's various writings, the various papers questioning whether univalent foundations are an improvement) are heterodox correctives in the opposite direction: they question whether type theoretic foundations are actually preferable to set theoretic foundations, and engaging with their arguments is valuable for learners who do not want to absorb type theoretic enthusiasm uncritically.

#### What to skip and why

Most introductory programming language books that touch on type theory should be skipped if the canonical type theoretic texts are available. The treatment of types in general programming language books is necessarily limited and does not develop the subject as type theory rather than as programming language feature.

Most "type-driven design" books that promise to teach type theoretic methodology without engaging with the underlying theory should be approached with caution. Some are useful methodological texts (Wlaschin's *Domain Modeling Made Functional* is genuinely good for what it covers), but they should not be confused with type theory study.

Most older type theory texts (from before approximately 2000) can be skipped as primary texts. The field has developed substantially, and contemporary texts cover the older material more clearly. Older papers remain valuable as primary sources for specific topics.

Most online type theory tutorials are uneven in quality, and the field's complexity makes shallow tutorials particularly unhelpful. The exceptions — the various lecture notes from active researchers, the documentation of major proof assistants, the well-maintained tutorial materials for Coq, Lean, and Agda — are valuable but not substitutes for systematic study.

Books that focus exclusively on category theoretic perspectives on type theory should be approached with caution unless one has substantial category theory background. The categorical perspective is illuminating but is not the appropriate primary entry for most learners.

#### Contemporary sources

The major venues for contemporary type theory research are POPL, ICFP, LICS (Logic in Computer Science), FSCD (Formal Structures for Computation and Deduction), and TYPES (the more specialized type theory workshop). Their proceedings represent the research frontier and are typically freely available.

The various Zulip chats and mailing lists associated with proof assistants are unusually valuable contemporary sources. The Lean Zulip is particularly active and welcomes newcomers. The Coq mailing list and Discourse are similarly active. Engagement with these communities is one of the most effective ways to stay current with the field.

Several blogs by active type theorists are valuable. Andrej Bauer's blog covers type theory and constructive mathematics with substantial accessibility. Mike Shulman's blog (and his contributions to the n-Category Café) covers homotopy type theory and categorical logic. Bob Harper's blog, mentioned above, provides opinionated commentary on type theory and programming language design. Phil Wadler's various essays and talks, available online, are valuable for understanding the broader intellectual context of type theory.

The HoTT community maintains substantial online resources, including the HoTT book itself, various tutorial materials, and the records of HoTT/UF workshops. The Univalent Foundations community is similarly well-organized.

For learners interested in mathematical formalization specifically, the various blogs and writings of mathematicians who have engaged with formalization (Kevin Buzzard's blog on Lean and mathematical formalization, Tom Hales's writings on the Flyspeck project, the various accounts of the formalization of major theorems) are valuable.

#### Reference table

| Resource | Role | Tag |
